Defining Opposition to Consensus
The author defines opposition to consensus using the Mu'tazilite example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defines opposition to consensus and why the Mu'tazilite position on the intermediate status is considered a violation.

Core Idea
Opposing consensus means introducing a view outside the range of existing legitimate opinions.

Before discussing how to handle differing opinions, the author first clarifies what it means to oppose consensus. Using the Mu'tazilite claim of an intermediate status between faith and disbelief as an example, he shows that adding a fourth position after the community had agreed on three constitutes clear opposition. This lesson establishes the foundational definition of consensus violation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on consensus opposition
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice how the author defines opposition by pointing to the three existing positions and the introduction of a fourth.
The example of the Mu'tazilite position is key.
2
Identify the three positions of the ummah
The author refers to three positions on the grave sinner before the Mu'tazilites. Can you guess what they were? (They are not stated explicitly but referenced.)
The three are: believer, disbeliever, or believer with sin.
3
Understand why the fourth position is opposition
The author argues that the ummah's agreement on three positions implies that any fourth is false. So creating a fourth is opposing the implied consensus.
This is a key point in Maturidi methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحقيقه ما هي المخالفة؟ فإن الأمة قبل ظهور نحلتهم إذا كانوا على ثلاثة أقوال كانوا مجتمعين على أن ما وراءها قول باطل، فهم إذا أحدثوا قولاً رابعا فقد خالفوا الإجماع، وكذا أجمعوا أن لا منزلة بين الإيمان والكفر، فمن أثبت المنزلة فقد خالف الأمة، والأخذ بالإجماع بما هو مخالف للإجماع من وجهين جهل فاحش. ثم الأمة إذا اختلفت في شيء على أقاويل لم يجز الخروج عن أقاويلهم لما مر أن ذلك منهم إجماع أن ما عدا هذه الأقاويل باطل، فبعد ذلك فالواجب البحث عن الأقاويل وعرضها على الدلائل ليتبين الصحيح من الفاسد والحق من الباطل، وعند العجز عن التمييز بين الحق منها والباطل يجب التوقف والرجوع إلى من أكرم بالعلم والخضوع له والجثو بين يديه ليبين له ذلك، فأما جعل التوقف الذي هو مقتضى تعارض الأدلة ونتيجة العجز عن ترجيح بعض الأقاويل على بعض موجبات الحيرة - مذهبًا يتمسك به وعقيدة يدان بها ويناضل عنها ويناظر مخالفوها فيحيد عما توجبه العقول وتقتضيه الأصول.
And the investigation: what is the opposition? For the ummah before the emergence of their sect, if they were upon three statements, they were united that beyond them is a false statement. So if they produce a fourth statement, they have opposed the consensus. And likewise, they agreed that there is no intermediate status between faith and disbelief. So whoever affirms the intermediate status has opposed the ummah. And adhering to consensus while opposing it in two ways is gross ignorance. Then, if the ummah differs on a matter into statements, it is not permissible to exit from their statements because of what has passed, that from them is a consensus that other than these statements is false. After that, the obligation is to investigate the statements and present them to the evidences so that the correct is distinguished from the corrupt and the truth from falsehood. And when unable to distinguish between the truth among them and falsehood, suspending judgment and returning to one who has been honored with knowledge, and submission to him, and kneeling before him so that he may clarify that for him, is obligatory. As for making suspending judgment, which is a requirement of conflicting evidences and the result of inability to prefer some statements over others, causes of confusion — a doctrine to be adhered to and a creed to be religiously held and defended and argued against its opponents, it deviates from what the intellects require and the principles necessitate.
Source pages p. 1049

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that their position on the intermediate status (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) is supported by consensus. The author begins by defining what constitutes opposition to consensus. He argues that before the Mu'tazilites appeared, the ummah held three positions on the status of a grave sinner: either he is a believer, or a disbeliever, or a believer with his sin. The Mu'tazilites introduced a fourth position — a status between belief and unbelief — thereby contradicting the implied consensus that any position outside the existing three is false. He also notes that the ummah had agreed that there is no intermediate status, so affirming one is opposing the ummah. Then he addresses the situation when legitimate differences occur: one cannot simply adopt a new opinion outside the existing range, because the existing range itself is a form of consensus that all other views are invalid. Instead, one must research these opinions and evaluate them against scriptural evidence to identify the truth. If one is incapable, suspension of judgment (tawaqquf) is required, along with consulting a qualified scholar. The author warns against erroneously turning tawaqquf into a permanent doctrine or creed, which is contrary to the demands of reason and the principles of the faith.

Key Terms
الإجماع
المخالفة
المنزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that consensus only applies to explicit agreement, not implied agreement.
Correction: The author shows that the ummah's de facto limitation to three positions amounts to an implicit consensus that excludes all others.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what counts as opposition to consensus?
A) Disagreeing with a single scholar
B) Introducing a view outside the range of existing opinions held by the ummah
C) Disagreeing with the caliph
D) Rejecting all previous scholarship

Key Takeaway
Opposing consensus is defined as introducing a view outside the range of positions the ummah has collectively held, as illustrated by the Mu'tazilite innovation of an intermediate status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever adopted a novel religious opinion because it seemed intellectually satisfying, without first checking whether it lies outside the consensus of the righteous predecessors? What would it cost you to let that opinion go?
?
The Mu'tazilites thought they were solving a theological problem, but their solution opposed the community. Are you willing to accept that some theological questions have no answer beyond what the early generations agreed upon, even if it feels incomplete to you?
?
If you knew that a view you hold was unknown to the first three generations of Muslims, would you have the humility to abandon it? What is stopping you from doing so today?

Obligation to Investigate Differing Opinions
The author prescribes the proper method for handling differing opinions and warns against permanent suspension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper methodology when encountering legitimate differences of opinion: investigate with evidence, and if unable, suspend judgment and seek knowledge from scholars, but avoid making tawaqquf a permanent doctrine.

Core Idea
When opinions differ, seek truth through evidence; if confused, pause and consult, but never turn confusion into a creed.

The second part of the passage moves from defining consensus violation to the correct approach when the ummah has legitimate differences. The author emphasizes that one cannot simply abandon the existing range of opinions; rather, one must research them and weigh them against evidence. If unable to decide, temporary suspension of judgment (tawaqquf) is allowed, but it should not be made into a permanent doctrine or used as an excuse for perpetual doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the section starting from 'ثم الأمة إذا اختلفت...' Understand the sequence: existing opinions form a consensus against new ones, then obligation to investigate, then obligation to consult if unable.
Note the contrast with the Mu'tazilite error.
2
Identify the two obligations
The author mentions two obligations: first, to investigate and present opinions to evidences; second, if unable, to suspend judgment and consult a scholar.
These are methodological rules.
3
Understand the warning about tawaqquf
The author warns against making tawaqquf a permanent doctrine or creed, as that would be a form of confusion contrary to reason and principles.
This is a critique of those who turn indecision into a position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحقيقه ما هي المخالفة؟ فإن الأمة قبل ظهور نحلتهم إذا كانوا على ثلاثة أقوال كانوا مجتمعين على أن ما وراءها قول باطل، فهم إذا أحدثوا قولاً رابعا فقد خالفوا الإجماع، وكذا أجمعوا أن لا منزلة بين الإيمان والكفر، فمن أثبت المنزلة فقد خالف الأمة، والأخذ بالإجماع بما هو مخالف للإجماع من وجهين جهل فاحش. ثم الأمة إذا اختلفت في شيء على أقاويل لم يجز الخروج عن أقاويلهم لما مر أن ذلك منهم إجماع أن ما عدا هذه الأقاويل باطل، فبعد ذلك فالواجب البحث عن الأقاويل وعرضها على الدلائل ليتبين الصحيح من الفاسد والحق من الباطل، وعند العجز عن التمييز بين الحق منها والباطل يجب التوقف والرجوع إلى من أكرم بالعلم والخضوع له والجثو بين يديه ليبين له ذلك، فأما جعل التوقف الذي هو مقتضى تعارض الأدلة ونتيجة العجز عن ترجيح بعض الأقاويل على بعض موجبات الحيرة - مذهبًا يتمسك به وعقيدة يدان بها ويناضل عنها ويناظر مخالفوها فيحيد عما توجبه العقول وتقتضيه الأصول.
And the investigation: what is the opposition? For the ummah before the emergence of their sect, if they were upon three statements, they were united that beyond them is a false statement. So if they produce a fourth statement, they have opposed the consensus. And likewise, they agreed that there is no intermediate status between faith and disbelief. So whoever affirms the intermediate status has opposed the ummah. And adhering to consensus while opposing it in two ways is gross ignorance. Then, if the ummah differs on a matter into statements, it is not permissible to exit from their statements because of what has passed, that from them is a consensus that other than these statements is false. After that, the obligation is to investigate the statements and present them to the evidences so that the correct is distinguished from the corrupt and the truth from falsehood. And when unable to distinguish between the truth among them and falsehood, suspending judgment and returning to one who has been honored with knowledge, and submission to him, and kneeling before him so that he may clarify that for him, is obligatory. As for making suspending judgment, which is a requirement of conflicting evidences and the result of inability to prefer some statements over others, causes of confusion — a doctrine to be adhered to and a creed to be religiously held and defended and argued against its opponents, it deviates from what the intellects require and the principles necessitate.
Source pages p. 1049

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that their position on the intermediate status (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) is supported by consensus. The author begins by defining what constitutes opposition to consensus. He argues that before the Mu'tazilites appeared, the ummah held three positions on the status of a grave sinner: either he is a believer, or a disbeliever, or a believer with his sin. The Mu'tazilites introduced a fourth position — a status between belief and unbelief — thereby contradicting the implied consensus that any position outside the existing three is false. He also notes that the ummah had agreed that there is no intermediate status, so affirming one is opposing the ummah. Then he addresses the situation when legitimate differences occur: one cannot simply adopt a new opinion outside the existing range, because the existing range itself is a form of consensus that all other views are invalid. Instead, one must research these opinions and evaluate them against scriptural evidence to identify the truth. If one is incapable, suspension of judgment (tawaqquf) is required, along with consulting a qualified scholar. The author warns against erroneously turning tawaqquf into a permanent doctrine or creed, which is contrary to the demands of reason and the principles of the faith.

Key Terms
التوقف
الدلائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that suspending judgment (tawaqquf) is a valid permanent position or a virtue.
Correction: The author explicitly warns that making tawaqquf a doctrine is a deviation, as it is only a temporary measure when evidence is unclear.

Checkpoint
What is the author's ruling on making tawaqquf a permanent doctrine?
A) It is recommended
B) It is obligatory when confused
C) It is a deviation from reason and principles
D) It is permissible only for scholars

Key Takeaway
When faced with differing opinions, investigate with evidence; if unable, seek a scholar, but never turn temporary indecision into a permanent creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The author says making suspension of judgment a permanent creed deviates from what the intellect requires. Have you made indecision a comfortable habit in your spiritual life? What will you do today to break that habit?

The Accusation of Breaking Threats and the Contradiction of General Verses
Nasafi responds to an accusation and points out the impossibility of both promise and threat verses being general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taking all promise and threat verses as absolutely general would lead to contradiction, and how this supports the Sunni position on forgiveness.

Core Idea
General verses of promise and threat cannot both be literal without contradiction.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi responds to an accusation made by opponents (likely the Mu'tazila) against Ahl al-Haqq. The opponents claim that by allowing forgiveness for major sins, the Sunnis are saying that God might break His threat. Nasafi calls this insolence and points out a deeper problem: both promise and threat verses are phrased as general statements. If taken literally and universally, they would contradict each other, which is impossible for divine speech. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic and the literal translation. Notice the two parts: the accusation and the logical argument.
The passage uses technical terms like 'khalaf fī al-waʿīd' and 'al-ʿumūm'.
2
Understand the accusation
The opponents accuse Ahl al-Haqq of affirming a break in the threat (khalaf fī al-waʿīd). What does that mean? They claim that if God can forgive a major sin without repentance, He would be breaking His threat of punishment.
Nasafi calls this accusation 'among the greatest insolences'.
3
Identify the contradiction
The author then points out that both promise verses (e.g., reward for good) and threat verses (e.g., punishment for evil) are phrased generally. If taken literally, a person with both good and evil deeds would simultaneously deserve reward and punishment, a contradiction.
This contradiction is impossible in God's speech and wisdom.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, these verses cannot be taken as absolutely general. Some interpretation or specification must apply, which supports the Sunni position that threats are conditional and forgiveness is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم هم ينسبون أهل الحق في قولهم بجواز العفو عن الكبيرة إلى إثبات الخلف في الوعيد ويشنعون عليهم بذلك وهذا من أعظم الوقاحات. ثم وردت آيات في الوعد خارجة على صيغة العموم وكذا آيات الوعيد، ولا وجه إلى إجراء الكل على العموم لما فيه من إثبات التناقض في آيات الله تعالى والتعارض في أدلته، وذلك خارج عن الحكمة.
Then they (the opponents) attribute to the People of Truth (Ahl al-Ḥaqq), in their statement permitting forgiveness for the major sin, the affirmation of a break in the threat (khalaf fī al-waʿīd) and they denounce them for that. This is among the greatest insolences. Then verses came concerning the promise (al-waʿd) phrased in a general form, and likewise verses concerning the threat (al-waʿīd). There is no way to apply all of them generally, because that would entail establishing contradiction in the verses of God Most High and conflict among His proofs, and that is outside wisdom.
Source pages p. 1052

Commentary
This passage addresses an accusation made by opponents (likely Mu'tazila) against Ahl al-Ḥaqq (the Sunnis). The opponents claim that by allowing the possibility of God forgiving a major sin without repentance (i.e., not necessarily carrying out the threat), the Sunnis are affirming that God might break His threat (al-waʿīd). The author calls this accusation extremely impudent (min aʿẓam al-waqāḥāt). Then he turns to a broader point about the Qur'anic verses: both verses of promise (al-waʿd) and verses of threat (al-waʿīd) are phrased in general terms (e.g., 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' and 'Whoever does an atom's weight of evil will see it'). If one were to take all such verses as absolutely general and unconditional, they would contradict each other, because a single person might have both good and evil deeds, leading to both reward and punishment simultaneously. This contradiction is impossible in God's speech and would be contrary to wisdom (al-ḥikma). Therefore, some interpretation must be applied to reconcile them.

Key Terms
أهل الحق (Ahl al-Ḥaqq)
الخلف في الوعيد (khalaf fī al-waʿīd)
العموم (al-ʿumūm)
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all general verses must be taken literally and unconditionally, especially threats.
Correction: Taking both promise and threat verses literally leads to contradiction, so interpretation is necessary. Threats are conditional in Sunni theology.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject taking all verses of promise and threat as general?
A) Because general verses are not authoritative.
B) Because it would create a contradiction in God's speech, which is impossible.
C) Because only verses of promise are general.
D) Because the verses are abrogated.

Key Takeaway
General verses of promise and threat cannot be taken literally without contradiction, therefore interpretation is required and threats are conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses of promise and threat in the Quran, do you find yourself applying them literally to yourself and others? How does this lesson challenge you to think more deeply about the balance between God's mercy and justice—and which one do you tend to emphasize in your daily hope and fear?
?
The opponents accused the People of Truth of insolence. Have you ever been quick to judge someone's belief as wrong without fully understanding their evidence? How can you guard against such pride in your own theological discussions, and what would it look like to truly listen before accusing?
?
If you were to die tonight with a major sin unrepented, what would be the basis of your hope in God's forgiveness? How does this lesson shape your understanding of that hope? Will you take God's threats seriously while also trusting in His mercy, or do you lean too heavily on one side? What one change will you make today in how you read the Quran's verses of promise and threat?

The Sectarian Dispute over Promise and Threat Verses
Nasafi reports two opposing sectarian positions on promise and threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila/Khawarij and Murji'a disagreed on whether verses of threat or promise should be taken as general.

Core Idea
Sects prioritized different verses based on their theological emphases.

Imam Nasafi presents the views of two major theological camps on how to interpret Quranic promises and threats. The Mu'tazila and Khawarij argue that threat verses are more general because they serve as stronger deterrents, while the Murji'a prioritize promise verses to align with God's mercy. This sets the stage for the Maturidi solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on sectarian views
Read the first two sentences of the Arabic passage carefully. They report the positions of the Mu'tazila/Khawarij and the Murji'a.
Notice the contrasting reasoning: deterrence vs. divine attributes.
2
Identify each group's reasoning
The Mu'tazila/Khawarij say threat verses are more effective as deterrence (zajr). The Murji'a say promise verses fit God's mercy and forgiveness.
3
Reflect on the theological root
Ask yourself: Why would these groups prioritize different verses? The answer lies in their views on God's justice, mercy, and human responsibility.
This background helps understand the Maturidi position in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم بعد ذلك اضطربت الأقاويل فزعمت المعتزلة والخوارج أن آيات الوعيد أحق بالعموم لما هو أبلغ في الزجر، وزعمت المرجئة أن آيات الوعد أحق بالعموم لأنه أحق بالذي عرف من صفات الله تعالى من الرحمة والعفو والغفران. والأصل عندنا أن ما كان من الآيات الواردة في الوعيد مقرونا بذكر الخلود فهو من المستحلّين لذلك، لما أنهم كفروا لاستحلال ذلك فأوعدوا على كفرهم في الحقيقة وذكر تلك الجريمة لكونها سببًا للكفر وطريقاً إليه، ولهذا قلنا في تأويل قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَلِدًا فِيهَا ﴾ أي متعمدًا الإماتة أي قتله لأجل أنه مؤمن ومن هذا قصده في القتل يكون كافرًا ألا ترى أنه حيث ذكر قتل العمد لا على الاستحلال فمن باب إيجاب القصاص بقي اسم الإيمان والأخوة بينه وبين المؤمنين وجعله أملاً للرحمة والتخفيف على ما بينا، فكان ذلك دليلاً أن هذه الآية وردت في المستحل وما يزعمون أن هذا وعيد للقتل لا
Then after that, the sayings became divergent. The Muʿtazila and the Khawārij claimed that the verses of threat are more entitled to be taken as general because they are more effective in deterrence. The Murjiʾa claimed that the verses of promise are more entitled to be general because they accord with what is known of God's attributes—mercy, pardon, and forgiveness. The fundamental principle with us is that whatever verses occur concerning the threat and are accompanied by mention of eternity (khulūd), they refer to those who make lawful (mustaḥillūn) that [sin], because they disbelieved by deeming it lawful, so they are threatened on account of their disbelief in reality, and that crime is mentioned because it is a cause of disbelief and a path to it. For this reason we said in interpreting the saying of God Most High: 'Whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding eternally therein'—i.e., intentionally causing death, i.e., killing him because he is a believer. Whoever intends that in killing becomes a disbeliever. Do you not see that where intentional killing is mentioned not on the basis of deeming it lawful, it falls under the ruling of obligatory retaliation (qiṣāṣ); the name of faith and brotherhood remains between him and the believers, and it is made a hope for mercy and alleviation, as we explained. So that was evidence that this verse came concerning the one who deems it lawful. And they claim that this is a threat for killing not...
Source pages p. 1053

Commentary
This passage first reports the positions of different sects on the interpretation of promise and threat verses. The Muʿtazila and Khawārij argue that verses of threat (al-waʿīd) are more appropriately taken as general because that provides stronger deterrence (zajr). The Murjiʾa argue that verses of promise (al-waʿd) are more appropriately general because they align with God's known attributes of mercy, pardon, and forgiveness. Then the author states the Maturidi position (al-aṣl ʿindanā). The key principle is that verses of threat that are accompanied by mention of eternity (khulūd) refer specifically to those who make the sin lawful (al-mustaḥillūn). The reason is that by deeming a sin lawful, they commit disbelief (kufr), so the eternal punishment is actually for their disbelief, not for the sin per se. The sin is mentioned only as the cause and means of that disbelief. The author then applies this to the famous verse on intentional killing of a believer (Quran 4:93). He interprets 'intentionally' (mutʿamiddan) as meaning killing him because of his faith, which is apostasy. To support this, he notes that elsewhere, when intentional killing is mentioned without the element of deeming it lawful (istiḥlāl), the ruling is only obligatory retaliation (qiṣāṣ), and the killer remains a believer, with hope of mercy and lighter punishment. This contrast shows that the verse with eternal punishment must be about the one who deems the killing lawful (i.e., an apostate). The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing to refute another interpretation.

Key Terms
المعتزلة (al-Muʿtazila)
الخوارج (al-Khawārij)
المرجئة (al-Murjiʾa)
آيات الوعيد (āyāt al-waʿīd)
آيات الوعد (āyāt al-waʿd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the dispute is arbitrary; it actually arises from different theological priorities.
Correction: Both sides have principled reasons based on their understanding of God's attributes and the purpose of revelation.

Checkpoint
Which group claimed that verses of threat are more entitled to be taken as general?
A) The Murjiʾa
B) The Muʿtazila and Khawārij
C) The Ashʿariyya
D) The Māturīdiyya

Key Takeaway
The Muʿtazila/Khawārij prioritized threat verses for deterrence; the Murjiʾa prioritized promise verses for mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses of punishment, do you instinctively apply them to others or to yourself? Which sect's approach does that reveal in you, and are you comfortable with that?
?
If God's mercy truly takes precedence over His wrath, as the Murji'a argue, what would it mean for you to live with confident hope rather than fear? What keeps you from that confidence?
?
Both groups had a rationale, yet they disagreed. What does your own heart lean toward when you read the Qur'an's warnings and promises? Is your understanding shaped more by God's attributes or by your own emotional state? How can you hold both in proper balance starting today?

The Maturidi Principle on Eternity in Threat Verses
Nasafi states the Maturidi principle interpreting threat verses that mention eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi principle that threat verses mentioning eternity refer to those who deem sin lawful (mustaḥillūn) and why.

Core Idea
Eternal punishment is for the disbelief of deeming sin lawful, not for the sin itself.

After presenting the conflicting sectarian views, Nasafi now states the Maturidi position. The key insight is that when Quranic threat verses include the word 'eternity' (khulūd), they are not directed at ordinary believers who commit sins, but at those who make that sin lawful (istaḥalla). This is because deeming a sin lawful constitutes disbelief, and the eternal punishment is actually for that disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi principle
Read the principle sentence carefully. It states that threat verses with eternity refer to the mustaḥillūn (those who deem sin lawful).
Underline the word 'mustaḥillūn'.
2
Understand the reasoning
Nasafi explains that by deeming the sin lawful, they disbelieve (kafarū). Thus, eternal punishment is for their disbelief in reality.
The sin is mentioned only as a cause or path to disbelief.
3
Reflect on the resolution
This principle resolves the sectarian dispute: threat verses with eternity apply to a specific category, not to all sinners.
This avoids both extremes.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So that was evidence that this verse came concerning the one who deems it lawful. And they claim that this is a threat for killing not...

Commentary
This passage first reports the positions of different sects on the interpretation of promise and threat verses. The Muʿtazila and Khawārij argue that verses of threat (al-waʿīd) are more appropriately taken as general because that provides stronger deterrence (zajr). The Murjiʾa argue that verses of promise (al-waʿd) are more appropriately general because they align with God's known attributes of mercy, pardon, and forgiveness. Then the author states the Maturidi position (al-aṣl ʿindanā). The key principle is that verses of threat that are accompanied by mention of eternity (khulūd) refer specifically to those who make the sin lawful (al-mustaḥillūn). The reason is that by deeming a sin lawful, they commit disbelief (kufr), so the eternal punishment is actually for their disbelief, not for the sin per se. The sin is mentioned only as the cause and means of that disbelief. The author then applies this to the famous verse on intentional killing of a believer (Quran 4:93). He interprets 'intentionally' (mutʿamiddan) as meaning killing him because of his faith, which is apostasy. To support this, he notes that elsewhere, when intentional killing is mentioned without the element of deeming it lawful (istiḥlāl), the ruling is only obligatory retaliation (qiṣāṣ), and the killer remains a believer, with hope of mercy and lighter punishment. This contrast shows that the verse with eternal punishment must be about the one who deems the killing lawful (i.e., an apostate). The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing to refute another interpretation.

Key Terms
الأصل (al-aṣl)
الخلود (al-khulūd)
المستحلّ (al-mustaḥill)
الاستحلال (al-istiḥlāl)
الكفر (al-kufr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think eternal punishment in Quran is for any major sin; Maturidis specify it's for those who deem it lawful.
Correction: The eternal threat is directed at the disbelief caused by deeming the sin lawful, not at the sin itself.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi's principle, what is the real reason for eternal punishment in threat verses that mention eternity?
A) The sin itself, because it is major.
B) The disbelief caused by deeming the sin lawful.
C) The lack of repentance.
D) Ignorance of the sin.

Key Takeaway
Threat verses with eternity are about disbelief through istiḥlāl, not ordinary sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered a sin you commit to be 'not that bad' or excusable? Reflect on where that thought might lead, and ask: do you risk falling into istiḥlāl by trivializing a sin that Allah has clearly forbidden? Commit today to call every sin by its name and never deem it lawful in your heart.
?
If you were to stand before Allah and He asked you about a sin you used to justify or minimize, what would you say? This lesson shows that the dangerous path is not the sin itself, but the attitude of making it permissible. Examine your own heart: is there any sin you have normalized? Resolve to repent from that mindset right now.
?
The Maturidi principle teaches that eternal punishment is for disbelief, not for the sin. But does this truth make you feel 'safe' in sinning, as long as you don't deem it lawful? That would be a terrible deception. Ask yourself: are you relying on technicalities to avoid accountability, or are you truly fearing Allah's punishment for every sin, major or minor? Let this question shake you into sincere taqwa.

Applying the Principle: Quran 4:93 on Intentional Killing
Nasafi applies the Maturidi principle to Quran 4:93 on intentional killing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi applies the Maturidi principle to the verse about intentional killing of a believer (Quran 4:93), interpreting it as referring to an apostate who deems murder lawful.

Core Idea
The eternal threat in 4:93 is for apostasy, not for murder alone.

Having established the principle that threat verses with eternity refer to mustaḥillūn, Nasafi now applies it to a specific controversial verse: 'Whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding eternally therein.' He argues that 'intentionally' here means killing him because of his faith, which makes the killer an apostate. To support this, he contrasts this verse with other verses where intentional killing is punished by qisas only, and the killer remains a believer.

Guided Path
1
Read the application passage
Read Nasafi's interpretation of Quran 4:93. He explains 'intentionally' as killing the believer because of his faith.
This interpretation makes the killer a mustaḥill and thus an apostate.
2
Analyze the contrast with qisas verses
Nasafi contrasts with verses where intentional killing is punished by qisas, where brotherhood and hope of mercy remain. This shows 4:93 is different.
The contrast proves the verse applies only to mustaḥill.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The verse's eternal threat is for the disbelief (istiḥlāl), not for the act of killing per se.
This safeguards the mercy of God for believers who sin.

Then after that, the sayings became divergent. The Muʿtazila and the Khawārij claimed that the verses of threat are more entitled to be taken as general because they are more effective in deterrence. So that was evidence that this verse came concerning the one who deems it lawful. And they claim that this is a threat for killing not...

Commentary
This passage first reports the positions of different sects on the interpretation of promise and threat verses. The Muʿtazila and Khawārij argue that verses of threat (al-waʿīd) are more appropriately taken as general because that provides stronger deterrence (zajr). The Murjiʾa argue that verses of promise (al-waʿd) are more appropriately general because they align with God's known attributes of mercy, pardon, and forgiveness. Then the author states the Maturidi position (al-aṣl ʿindanā). The key principle is that verses of threat that are accompanied by mention of eternity (khulūd) refer specifically to those who make the sin lawful (al-mustaḥillūn). The reason is that by deeming a sin lawful, they commit disbelief (kufr), so the eternal punishment is actually for their disbelief, not for the sin per se. The sin is mentioned only as the cause and means of that disbelief. The author then applies this to the famous verse on intentional killing of a believer (Quran 4:93). This contrast shows that the verse with eternal punishment must be about the one who deems the killing lawful (i.e., an apostate). The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing to refute another interpretation.

Refutation of Three Sects on Forgiveness
Refutes three sects' views on divine forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila, Kharijites, and Murji'a views on forgiveness are unsound, and the proper definition of forgiveness as passing over one who deserves punishment.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is passing over one who deserves punishment, not a reward for the sinless.

This passage tackles a critical theological disagreement about God's forgiveness. Three sects distort the meaning of forgiveness by denying that believers can deserve punishment. The author sets the record straight by defining forgiveness and showing the logical consequences of their positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument come through before interpreting.
Notice the clear definition of forgiveness in the middle.
2
Identify the three sects and their views
Find where the Mu'tazila, Kharijites, and Murji'a are mentioned. Note each one's view on forgiveness according to the passage.
The Mu'tazila restrict forgiveness to minor sins; the Kharijites and Murji'a have different implications.
3
Understand the definition of forgiveness
Focus on the sentence: 'إذ المغفرة التجاوز عمن يجوز تعذيبه'. This is the key definition. Forgiveness only makes sense if the person could otherwise be punished.
Contrast with permissible acts, where no punishment is due.
4
See how each sect's view fails
Apply the definition to each sect. The Mu'tazila limit forgiveness to minor sins, but the definition requires possibility of punishment. The Kharijites and Mu'tazila cannot conceive any believer deserving punishment, so forgiveness is impossible. The Murji'a only see disbelievers as punishable, so forgiveness is irrelevant.
Only Ahl al-Sunna maintains that believers may sin and be forgiven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحمل المعتزلة ذلك على مغفرة الصغائر لمن اجتنب الكبائر غير سديد، إذ المغفرة التجاوز عمن يجوز تعذيبه على ما ارتكب فأما ترك تعذيب من لا يجوز تعذيبه فليس بعفو ولا مغفرة كترك التعذيب على المباحات. وتقرر بالوقوف على هذه المعاني أن لا تحقق لكونه تعالى غافرًا غفورا رحيمًا على أصل الخوارج والمعتزلة إذ لا يتصور عندهم مغفرة من يجوز له تعذيبه ولا على أصل المرجئة، فإن عندهم ما يجوز له إلا تعذيب الكافر والعفو عنه غير ثابت.
And the Mu'tazila have interpreted that as forgiveness of minor sins for one who avoids major sins – an unsound interpretation. For forgiveness is passing over one whose punishment is permissible for what he committed. As for leaving off punishment of one whose punishment is not permissible, that is not pardon nor forgiveness, like leaving off punishment for permissible acts. And it is established, by pausing upon these meanings, that there is no realization that He, Exalted, is al-Ghafir, al-Ghafur, al-Rahim according to the doctrine of the Kharijites and Mu'tazila, since they cannot conceive of forgiveness for one whose punishment is permissible. Nor according to the doctrine of the Murji'a, for according to them, the only one whose punishment is permissible is the disbeliever, and pardon for him is not established.
Source pages p. 1055

Commentary
This passage refutes the interpretations of three Islamic sects regarding God's forgiveness. First, it rejects the Mu'tazila view that forgiveness (maghfira) applies only to minor sins (sagha'ir) for those who avoid major sins (kaba'ir). The author argues that this is unsound because true forgiveness is 'passing over' someone who deserves punishment; if a person does not deserve punishment at all (like one who only does permissible acts), then it is not forgiveness. Second, it examines the positions of the Kharijites and Mu'tazila: according to them, no believer can deserve punishment for sin (since they hold that a sinner becomes a disbeliever or is in a state between faith and disbelief), so they cannot conceive of God forgiving a believer who deserves punishment. Third, it addresses the Murji'a, who hold that only disbelievers deserve punishment; thus, pardon for them is not established because they already do not deserve punishment. Hence, the valid position is the Ahl al-Sunna view that believers can commit sins less than kufr, may be punished, and then may be forgiven by God's grace.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الخوارج
المرجئة
المغفرة
الصغائر
الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forgiveness is only for minor sins, or that forgiveness can apply to those who do not deserve punishment.
Correction: True forgiveness requires that the person could be justly punished; otherwise it is not forgiveness but simply refraining from punishing the innocent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the Mu'tazila interpretation of forgiveness as unsound?
A) Because they limit forgiveness to major sins
B) Because forgiveness applies only to those who deserve punishment, not to those who do not
C) Because they deny God's names
D) Because they claim forgiveness is for disbelievers

Key Takeaway
True forgiveness requires that the person punished could have been justly punished; only the Ahl al-Sunna view maintains this for believers, preserving God's attributes of forgiveness and mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that you deserve punishment, or do you assume God's mercy automatically covers you without any need for repentance? How does the definition of forgiveness in this passage challenge your comfort?
?
In your view, does God's forgiveness require you to be deserving of punishment? How does that affect your relationship with your sins—do you take them lightly or treat them as serious?
?
Which of the three sects' views do you find yourself subtly leaning toward in your daily life: thinking that a believer cannot deserve punishment (like the Kharijites/Mu'tazila) or that only disbelievers deserve it (like the Murji'a)? How can you correct that in your heart to align with the Ahl al-Sunna understanding?

Refuting Khawarij with Prophets' Sins
The author refutes Khawarij by arguing that prophets' sins were not kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the example of prophets' sins to refute the Khawarij's argument that major sins cause disbelief.

Core Idea
Prophets' sins show disobedience doesn't equal disbelief.

The Khawarij argued that any major sinner is a disbeliever based on Qur'an 4:14, which threatens eternal punishment for disobedience. The author challenges them to apply this logic to the prophets, whose slips are mentioned in the Qur'an. This forces the Khawarij into a dilemma that exposes the flaw in their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full flow of the argument. Notice how the author poses two conditional questions to the Khawarij.
The passage uses a dialectical style common in kalam.
2
Identify the dilemma
The author asks the Khawarij: Were the prophets' slips disobedience or not? Each answer leads to a contradiction. Trace how both options are problematic.
If they say no, they contradict the Qur'an; if yes, they must either call prophets disbelievers or abandon their proof.
3
Understand the reinterpretation
After the dilemma, the author argues that the verse actually refers to the disbeliever who considers sin permissible, not to the believing sinner. Note the linguistic points about 'limits' and 'most wretched'.
This reinterpretation is consistent with the Maturidi position that faith remains despite major sins.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that faith is not nullified by major sins, and the verse about eternal punishment applies only to disbelievers. This directly refutes the Khawarij proof.
The conclusion rests on both scriptural and rational arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مناقشة رأي الخوارج: وتعلق الخوارج بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَعْصِ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَهُ﴾ الآية. قلنا لهم: ما تقولون في زلات الأنبياء عليهم السلام أنها هل كانت تسمى عصيانا؟ فإن قالوا: لا. كذبوا الله تعالى في قوله: ﴿ وَعَصَى عَادَمُ رَبَّهُ، فَغَوَى ﴾ ونسبوه إلى الظلم حيث عاتب داود وغيره من الأنبياء على ما وجد منهم من الزلات وأبطلوا مغفرته لداود بقوله: ﴿ فَغَفَرْنَا لَهُ ذَلِكَ ﴾ وذلك كفر. وإن قالوا: كان ذلك منهم عصيانًا قيل: فهل كفروا واستحقوا الخلود في النار؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. كفروا. وإن قالوا: لا. أبطلوا دليلهم. ثم الآية مصروفة إلى الاستحلال على أن في الآية دليلاً أنها وردت في الكافر لأنه قال: ويتعد حدوده، والحدود اسم جمع والمؤمن لا يتعدى جميع حدود الله. وقوله: ﴿ لَا يَصْلَهَا إِلَّا الأَشْقَى ﴾ أي على طريق الإصالة. وكذا قوله: ﴿ أُعِدَّتْ للكفرين ﴾ على طريق الإصالة. والله أعلم. على أنا بينا بما تلونا من الآيات وذكرنا من المعقول أن الإيمان لا ينعدم بالكبيرة، ولا بما دون التكذيب من العصيان، ولا خلود مع الإيمان، فكان ذلك دليلاً أن هذه الآية وردت في الكافر.
Discussion of the opinion of the Khawarij: And the Khawarij clung to His saying: 'And whoever disobeys Allah and His Messenger' the verse. We said to them: What do you say about the slips of the prophets, peace be upon them, were they called disobedience? If they say: No. They lie against Allah in His saying: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord, so he erred.' And they attribute injustice to Him where He rebuked David and other prophets for what occurred of slips from them, and they nullify His forgiveness for David by His saying: 'So We forgave him that,' and that is disbelief. If they say: That was disobedience from them, it is said: Then did they disbelieve and deserve eternity in the Fire? If they say: Yes. They disbelieve. If they say: No. They nullify their proof. Then the verse is directed to [the one who] considers [sin] permissible; for in the verse is evidence that it came [revealed] about the disbeliever, because He said: 'and transgresses His limits,' and 'limits' is a collective noun, and the believer does not transgress all of Allah's limits. And His saying: 'None will enter it except the most wretched' meaning in an original way. And likewise His saying: 'Prepared for the disbelievers' in an original way. And Allah knows best. Moreover, we have shown by what we have recited of the verses and mentioned of rational arguments that faith is not nullified by a major sin, nor by disobedience less than denial, and there is no eternal [punishment] with faith, so that was evidence that this verse came [revealed] about the disbeliever.
Source pages p. 1056

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of the Khawarij who used Qur'an 4:14 (about eternal punishment for disobedience) to argue that major sinners become disbelievers. The author poses a dilemma: If the Khawarij deny that the prophets' slips (like Adam and David) were disobedience, they contradict the Qur'an and accuse God of injustice. If they admit they were disobedience, then they must either say the prophets disbelieved and deserve eternal fire (which is impossible) or admit that disobedience does not necessarily entail disbelief, thereby undermining their proof. The author then argues that the verse actually refers to the disbeliever, because it speaks of transgression of all limits (which a believer does not do) and describes the ultimate outcome as eternity in Hell for the most wretched (al-ashqa), which is the disbeliever. He concludes that faith is not nullified by major sins, echoing the Maturidi position on the relation of faith and sin.

Key Terms
الخوارج
زلات الأنبياء
العصيان
الكفر
الخلود
الكبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any major sin makes a person a disbeliever who will be eternally punished.
Correction: Prophets committed sins but remained believers; the verse applies to those who consider sin permissible, not to believers.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the Khawarij's use of the verse about disobedience?
A) The verse abrogates all previous revelations.
B) The verse only applies to prophets.
C) The Khawarij cannot consistently hold that prophets' sins were not disobedience without lying about the Qur'an.
D) The verse is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij's proof fails because it leads to absurd conclusions about the prophets, and the verse actually refers to disbelievers, not believing sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you secretly believe that committing a major sin takes someone out of faith, what does this passage force you to reconsider about your own judgment of others? Will you now hesitate before calling any Muslim a disbeliever?
?
The prophets made mistakes yet remained beloved to Allah. How does this change the way you view your own sins? Are you willing to turn to Allah in repentance without falling into despair that you've lost your faith?
?
When you read verses that threaten eternal punishment, do you instinctively apply them to others or to yourself? This lesson shows that such verses are for those who declare sin permissible. Are you tempted to label other Muslims as disbelievers based on their sins? What accountability does that place on you?

The General-Specific Debate and Its Theological Stakes
Al-Nasafi describes the early Hanafi-Mu'tazilite debate on general/specific and its connection to wa'id and 'afw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate over general and specific terms in the Quran was linked to the Mu'tazilite-Hanafi dispute about divine punishment and forgiveness.

Core Idea
The general-specific debate determined whether God's threats are unconditional or allow for forgiveness.

This passage from Al-Nasafi takes us into the heart of an early theological debate: how should we interpret general statements in the Quran? The answer had direct consequences for whether God must punish sinners or may forgive them. As you read, pay attention to how each group’s linguistic reasoning led to a different theological conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's description of the debate come through.
Notice the strong language: 'they used to debate most severely'.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the Hanafi position on general/specific and the Mu'tazilite position. What does each group say about a later specification?
The Hanafis call it 'clarification' (bayan), the Mu'tazila call it 'abrogation' (naskh).
3
Connect to wa'id and 'afw
The passage says both groups linked this linguistic issue to the threat of punishment and forgiveness. Reflect on why that link exists.
If general threats are unconditional, punishment is certain. If they can be specified, forgiveness is possible.
4
Review the key terms
Review the key terms like 'umum, mutlaq, muqayyad, wa'id, 'afw. Understanding these is essential for following the argument.
5
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question based only on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان أوائل أصحابنا يتنازعونهم في هذا الأصل أشد المنازعة، ولا يسلمون لهم أن القول بالعموم واجب الاعتقاد لما أن التكلم بلفظة العموم يراد بها الخصوص سائغ فيما بين أهل اللسان حتى كان ذلك يغلب على إرادة الحقيقة وهو العموم، وكذا اللفظ العاري عن القيد يجوز أن يكون المراد به القيد عند مشايخنا، وهم يأبون ذلك أشد الإباء ويجعلون دليل التخصيص أو القيد إذا كان متأخرا عن العام أو المطلق نسخاً لا بيانًا أن المتكلم أراد به من أول ما تكلم ما وراء ما دخل تحت القيد ودليل الخصوص، وأصحابنا كانوا يجعلون ذلك بيانًا وكان رحمهم الله — الفريقان جميعًا يجعلون هذا الأصل من نتائج مسألة الوعيد، وكان كل من تكلم من أو ائلنا وأوائلهم في مسألة العام والمطلق يذكرون أن القول بها يؤدي إلى القول بتحقيق الوعيد أو بجواز العفو حتى إن أبا بكر الجصاص قال بعد ما استشهد
And the early ones among our companions used to debate them on this principle most severely, and they would not concede to them that affirming the general is obligatory to believe, because speaking a general term while intending the specific is permissible among the people of the language, even to the extent that it overpowers the intention of the literal meaning, which is generality. Similarly, the unqualified term may be intended as qualified according to our masters. But they (the Mu'tazila) reject that most vehemently and consider the indicator of specification or qualification, if it occurs after the general or absolute, to be abrogation, not clarification, claiming that the speaker intended from the beginning what is beyond the scope of the qualification and the indicator of specification. Our companions considered that to be clarification. Both groups considered this principle to be a result of the issue of threat (wa'id). And everyone who spoke among our early ones and their early ones regarding the issue of the general and the absolute would mention that affirming one leads to affirming the realization of the threat or the permissibility of forgiveness, until Abu Bakr al-Jassas said after he had cited...
Source pages p. 1059

Commentary
This passage describes the historical debate between the early Hanafi scholars (ashabuna) and the Mu'tazila concerning the interpretation of general (amm) and specific (khas) in the Quran. The author states that the early Hanafis strongly disputed the Mu'tazilite claim that the general must be believed and applied without exception. The Hanafis argued that using a general term while intending the specific is permissible in Arabic, and in fact often the intended meaning is the specific rather than the literal general. Similarly, an unqualified term can be intended as qualified. The Mu'tazila rejected this, arguing that if a specification comes after the general, it is abrogation of the original meaning, not a clarification of the intended meaning from the start. The Hanafis insisted it is a clarification. Despite their differences, both groups agreed that this issue of general/specific is linked to the doctrine of threat (wa'id) and forgiveness ('afw). The Mu'tazila held that if the general threats are not literally fulfilled, that would contradict God's truthfulness, so they insisted on the realization of the threat. The Hanafis allowed specification, which leaves room for God's forgiveness. The author mentions that Abu Bakr al-Jassas also addressed this.

Key Terms

عموم
مطلق
مقيد
وعيد
عفو
نسخ
بيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The debate over general and specific terms is purely linguistic and has no theological implications.
Correction: The passage shows that both Hanafis and Mu'tazila considered this issue to be directly linked to the doctrine of threat (wa'id) and forgiveness ('afw), making it a central theological dispute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the Mu'tazila consider a subsequent specification of a general statement to be?
A) Clarification (bayan)
B) Abrogation (naskh)
C) Interpretation (ta'wil)
D) Allegory (majaz)

Key Takeaway
The debate over general and specific terms in revelation was not merely linguistic: it directly determined whether God's threats of punishment are unconditional or can be mitigated by divine forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses in the Quran that promise severe punishment for sins, do you instinctively assume that punishment is your certain fate, or do you immediately recall that God's mercy is vast and can override His threats? Which of these two responses reflects your actual state of fear and hope? Will you consciously train your heart to hold both realities together, so that fear drives you to obedience and hope lifts you from despair?
?
The early scholars disagreed fiercely on whether God's later verses of mercy clarify His earlier threats or change them. In your own journey with Allah, have you ever struggled to reconcile verses of strict justice with verses of boundless mercy? How will you let the understanding that mercy is the clarification of God's ultimate intent shape your relationship with Him starting today?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted that God must follow through on His threats, while the Hanafis left room for His forgiveness. Which tendency do you see more in your heart—demanding strict justice for yourself and others, or hoping for mercy? How might this tendency affect your character and your dealings with people? Will you ask Allah to grant you the balance of fearing His justice while hoping for His mercy?

The Mu'tazila Argument on Generality and Specification
Mu'tazila argument on specification and its link to abrogation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position that general expressions must be taken as general unless accompanied by connected evidence, and that later specification constitutes abrogation.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, a general expression is only specified by connected evidence; later restriction is abrogation, not clarification.

This lesson introduces the Mu'tazila argument on 'umum (generality). They held that a general word, when used with its specific name, cannot be specified without an accompanying evidence. If such evidence is absent, the word remains general, and any later exclusion of some individuals would be a form of abrogation, which is impossible in divine reports. This position has profound implications for understanding divine threats and promises.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Read the first part of the passage where the author states the Mu'tazila view. Notice how they define specification and its relation to evidence.
The argument hinges on the concept of connected (muttasil) vs. separate (munfasil) evidence.
2
Identify the key condition for specification
Find the condition that specification requires an evidence, and when that evidence is absent, the formula stands as general.
3
Distinguish between commands/prohibitions and reports
Note that the Mu'tazila apply this reasoning to commands and prohibitions, but not to reports, because a report would become false if later restricted.
This distinction is crucial for their theology.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this view leads to affirming divine threats without possibility of pardon, a key point in the debate with the Maturidis.
This connects to the broader issue of wa'id.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعينه باسمه الخاص وما هذا سبيله فلا وجه إلى القول بتخصيصه إذ التخصيص هو دليل يدل أن المخصوص غير داخل فى العموم، وذلك لن يكون إلا بدليل متصل وانعدم، وعند انعدامه بقيت الصيغة متعرية عن الدليل وهي عند تعريها دليل إرادة للعموم، فبعد ذلك إخراج بعض ما تناوله العموم يكون نسخا وإنهاء للحكم الثابت فيه إلى هذه المدة، وهذا متصور في الأوامر والنواهي فأما في الأخبار فغير يصير الإخبار كذبًا وهو مستحيل على الله تعالى، لم يزل المعتزلة يتمسكون بهذا الكلام. وكان أوائل أصحابنا يتنازعونهم في هذا الأصل أشد المنازعة، ولا يسلمون لهم أن القول بالعموم واجب الاعتقاد لما أن التكلم بلفظة العموم يراد بها الخصوص سائغ فيما بين أهل اللسان حتى كان ذلك يغلب على إرادة الحقيقة وهو العموم، وكذا اللفظ العاري عن القيد يجوز أن يكون المراد به القيد عند مشايخنا، وهم يأبون ذلك أشد الإباء ويجعلون دليل التخصيص أو القيد إذا كان متأخرا عن العام أو المطلق نسخاً لا بيانًا أن المتكلم أراد به من أول ما تكلم ما وراء ما دخل تحت القيد ودليل الخصوص، وأصحابنا كانوا يجعلون ذلك بيانًا وكانوا رحمهم الله — الفريقان جميعًا يجعلان هذا الأصل من نتائج مسألة الوعيد، وكان كل من تكلم من أوائلنا وأوائلهم في مسألة العام والمطلق يذكرون أن القول بها يؤدي إلى القول بتحقيق الوعيد أو بجواز العفو حتى إن أبا بكر الجصاص قال بعد ما استشهد لصحة قوله فبان بما وصفنا أن العموم من مفهوم لسان العرب، وأن ذلك من مذهب السلف من غير خلاف بينهم وما خالف من هذا أحد من السلف ومن بعدهم إلى أن نشأت فرقة من المرجئة ضاق عليهم المذهب في القول بالإرجاء فلجأت إلى دفع القول بالعموم رأسًا لئلا يلزمها لخصومها القول بوعيد الفساق بظاهر الآي المقتضية لذلك. هذا كله لفظه ذكره في كتابه "المُصنف في أصول الفقه" وقال أيضا في أول هذا الباب وحكى لي أبو الطيب بن شهاب عن أبي الحسن الكرخي أنه قال : إني أقف في عموم الأخبار وأقول بالعموم في الأمر والنهي، فقلت لأبي الطيب فهذا يدل على أن مذهبه كان الوقف في وعيد فساق أهل الملة، فقال : هكذا كان مذهبه، قال الجصاص وأبو الطيب هذا غير مهم عندي فيما يحكيه، وقد جالس أبا سعيد البردعي وشيوخنا القدماء ولم أسمع أن أبا الحسن الكرخي يفرق بين الخير والأمر والنهي في ذلك بل كان يقول بالعموم والإطلاق.
By its specific name, and what is on this path, there is no justification for saying it is specified, for specification is an evidence indicating that the specified is not included in the generality, and that cannot be except with a connected evidence, and it is absent; and when it is absent, the formula remains bare of evidence, and when it is bare, it is evidence of intending generality. Therefore, after that, excluding some of what the generality encompasses is abrogation and termination of the established ruling in it until that time. This is conceivable in commands and prohibitions. As for reports, it is not [conceivable] because the report becomes a lie, and that is impossible for God Almighty. The Mu'tazila have always clung to this speech. And the early ones of our companions used to dispute with them on this principle most severely, and they would not concede to them that the statement of generality is obligatory to believe, because speaking a general word intending a specific meaning is permissible among the people of the language, to the extent that this often overcomes the intended reality which is generality. Likewise, the word bare of restriction, it is permissible that the intended meaning is the restriction according to our masters, but they (the Mu'tazila) reject that most severely and make the evidence of specification or restriction, if it comes after the general or absolute, abrogation, not clarification, [claiming] that the speaker intended from the first time he spoke what is beyond the restriction and the evidence of specificity. And our companions used to consider that clarification, and they — may God have mercy on them — both groups all consider this principle to be from the results of the issue of threat (wa'id). And everyone who spoke from our early ones and their early ones regarding the issue of the general and the absolute used to mention that the statement on it leads to the statement of actualizing the threat or allowing pardon, until Abu Bakr al-Jassas said, after he adduced evidence for the correctness of his statement, 'It became clear by what we described that generality is from the concept of the language of the Arabs, and that is from the doctrine of the predecessors without disagreement among them. And no one among the predecessors or after them opposed this until a group from the Murji'a arose, [for whom] the doctrine on irja' became constrained, so they resorted to rejecting the statement on generality entirely, lest their opponents force upon them the statement of the threat of sinners by the apparent verses that necessitate that.' All this is his wording, mentioned in his book 'al-Musannaf fi Usul al-Fiqh'. And he also said at the beginning of this chapter: Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab narrated to me from Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi that he said: 'I stop (waqafa) regarding the generality of reports, and I affirm generality in command and prohibition.' I said to Abu al-Tayyib: 'This indicates that his doctrine was stopping on the threat of sinful people of the religion.' He said: 'That was his doctrine.' Al-Jassas said: 'Abu al-Tayyib is not important to me in what he narrates; he used to frequent Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i and our ancient masters, and I did not hear that Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi distinguished between report and command and prohibition in that; rather, he used to affirm generality and absoluteness.'
Source pages p. 1059

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argumentative discussion about whether general (عام) expressions in scripture can be intended to mean specific (خاص) things, and how this relates to the theological issue of divine threat (الوعيد) and pardon (العفو). The author begins by stating the Mu'tazila position: if a general statement is given with its specific name, there is no basis for saying it is specified (تخصيص) because specification requires a separate evidence; in its absence the statement remains proof of generality. Excluding some parts would then be abrogation (نسخ), not clarification (بيان), and this applies to commands and prohibitions, but not to reports (since that would entail falsehood, impossible for God). The Mu'tazila continuously rely on this reasoning. Then the author explains that the early Maturidi/Hanafi scholars (أوائل أصحابنا) strongly opposed the Mu'tazila on this principle. They did not accept that affirming generality is obligatory in belief, because it is permissible in Arabic for a general expression to intend a specific meaning, and similarly for an unrestricted word to intend a restriction. However, the Mu'tazila reject this and treat delayed specification as abrogation rather than clarification, claiming the speaker intended the restricted meaning from the outset. The Maturidi scholars, by contrast, considered such delayed evidence as clarification (بيان) of the original intention. Next, the author notes that both groups linked this issue to the problem of al-wa'id: the threat of punishment for sinners. Those who affirm generality tend to uphold the threat (i.e., that the revealed threats apply to all sinners), while those who allow specification (takhsis) leave room for pardon (عفو). He then quotes Abu Bakr al-Jassas, who argued that generality is part of Arabic usage and was the view of the Salaf, and that only the Murji'a later denied generality to avoid having to affirm the threat of punishment for sinners. Al-Jassas also reports a narration from Abu al-Tayyib about Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi: al-Karkhi allegedly said he stops (يقف) on the generality of reports (i.e., does not affirm it) but affirms it for commands and prohibitions. Al-Jassas comments that he does not consider Abu al-Tayyib reliable in this report, and that he personally knows that al-Karkhi did not distinguish between reports and commands in this matter; rather, al-Karkhi affirmed generality and absoluteness (الإطلاق) across the board. The passage thus presents a complex interweaving of linguistic-legal theory (usul al-fiqh) with theological doctrine (kalam), showing how seemingly neutral technical rules about textual interpretation have direct implications for beliefs about God's promises and threats.

الخصوص
التخصيص
النسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that specification always requires separate evidence that is delayed.
Correction: The Mu'tazila insist that the evidence must be connected (mutall bil-'umum), not delayed; otherwise it is abrogation.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila argument, what happens if a general expression is later specified without a connected evidence?
A) It is considered a clarification of the original intention
B) It is considered abrogation of the ruling
C) It is considered a lie in reports
D) It is considered permissible in all cases

Key Takeaway
For the Mu'tazila, without connected evidence, a general expression must be taken as general, and any later restriction is abrogation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear command from the Qur'an or Sunnah by assuming it was 'only for a specific time' without any evidence from the time itself? How does the Mu'tazila argument about connected evidence challenge you to take the plain meaning of Allah's speech more seriously unless there is compelling reason connected to it?
?
The Mu'tazila insist that changing a report would make it a lie, impossible for Allah. When you read verses that promise reward or warn of punishment, do you treat them as true literally or do you mentally soften them? What would it mean for your life to fully trust that every report from Allah is exactly as He intended?
?
If you believe that later rulings can abrogate earlier ones (like in the Qur'an), does this make you feel free to pick and choose? How can you ensure you are not using 'abrogation' lightly to avoid obligations that still apply to you?

Early Maturidi Refutation of the Mu'tazila View
Early Maturidi refutation of Mu'tazila on 'umum and takhsis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how early Maturidi scholars argued that general expressions can be intended to mean specific things, and that delayed specification is clarification, not abrogation.

Core Idea
The Maturidis held that Arabic allows a general word to intend a specific meaning, making later evidence a clarification, not abrogation.

The early Maturidi scholars (أوائل أصحابنا) strongly contested the Mu'tazila view on generality. They argued that it is permissible in Arabic for a general expression to be intended for a specific meaning, and similarly for an unrestricted word to be intended for a restricted one. Consequently, a later evidence that specifies the general is not abrogation but a clarification (bayan) of what was originally intended. This difference is tied directly to the theological issue of wa'id (divine threat).

Guided Path
1
Read the Maturidi counter-argument
Read the section where the author describes the early Maturidi response. Notice how they affirm the linguistic possibility of specification.
Contrast this with the Mu'tazila position from the previous lesson.
2
Identify the key linguistic claim
The Maturidis claim that in Arabic, a general word can be used to intend a specific meaning, and this is common.
They also say an unrestricted word can be intended with a restriction.
3
Understand the difference between bayan and naskh
For the Mu'tazila, delayed evidence is naskh (abrogation). For the Maturidis, it is bayan (clarification). Note why this matters.
4
Link to wa'id
The author states that both groups consider this principle a result of the issue of threat. Reflect on how allowing specification opens the door for pardon (afw).

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argumentative discussion about whether general (عام) expressions in scripture can be intended to mean specific (خاص) things, and how this relates to the theological issue of divine threat (الوعيد) and pardon (العفو). The author begins by stating the Mu'tazila position: if a general statement is given with its specific name, there is no basis for saying it is specified (تخصيص) because specification requires a separate evidence; in its absence the statement remains proof of generality. Excluding some parts would then be abrogation (نسخ), not clarification (بيان), and this applies to commands and prohibitions, but not to reports (since that would entail falsehood, impossible for God). The Mu'tazila continuously rely on this reasoning. Then the author explains that the early Maturidi/Hanafi scholars (أوائل أصحابنا) strongly opposed the Mu'tazila on this principle. They did not accept that affirming generality is obligatory in belief, because it is permissible in Arabic for a general expression to intend a specific meaning, and similarly for an unrestricted word to intend a restriction. However, the Mu'tazila reject this and treat delayed specification as abrogation rather than clarification, claiming the speaker intended the restricted meaning from the outset. The Maturidi scholars, by contrast, considered such delayed evidence as clarification (بيان) of the original intention. Next, the author notes that both groups linked this issue to the problem of al-wa'id: the threat of punishment for sinners. Those who affirm generality tend to uphold the threat (i.e., that the revealed threats apply to all sinners), while those who allow specification (takhsis) leave room for pardon (عفو). He then quotes Abu Bakr al-Jassas, who argued that generality is part of Arabic usage and was the view of the Salaf, and that only the Murji'a later denied generality to avoid having to affirm the threat of punishment for sinners. Al-Jassas also reports a narration from Abu al-Tayyib about Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi: al-Karkhi allegedly said he stops (يقف) on the generality of reports (i.e., does not affirm it) but affirms it for commands and prohibitions. Al-Jassas comments that he does not consider Abu al-Tayyib reliable in this report, and that he personally knows that al-Karkhi did not distinguish between reports and commands in this matter; rather, al-Karkhi affirmed generality and absoluteness (الإطلاق) across the board. The passage thus presents a complex interweaving of linguistic-legal theory (usul al-fiqh) with theological doctrine (kalam), showing how seemingly neutral technical rules about textual interpretation have direct implications for beliefs about God's promises and threats.

Key Terms
العموم
الخصوص
التخصيص
البيان
النسخ
الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidis completely reject the concept of generality in favor of pure specificity.
Correction: They accept generality as a linguistic possibility but allow for specification based on Arabic usage, making later evidence a clarification.

Checkpoint
According to the early Maturidi view, what is a delayed evidence of specification considered?
A) Abrogation (naskh)
B) Clarification (bayan)
C) A lie
D) A new ruling

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis argue that later specification is a clarification, not an abrogation, because Arabic allows general words to intend specific meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of threat (wa'id) in the Quran, do you immediately assume it applies to you? How does the Maturidi principle that such verses can be specified change your personal sense of accountability?
?
Think of a time when you misunderstood a religious text, only later to gain clarity. Did you view your earlier understanding as 'abrogated' or simply 'clarified'? What does that reveal about your humility in seeking truth?
?
The Maturidis and Mu'tazila both considered themselves sincere Muslims, yet they disagreed on a fundamental linguistic principle. How does this challenge your tendency to dismiss those with differing views? Are you willing to accept that your own interpretations may be subject to specification?

The Link to Wa'id and Abu Bakr al-Jassas' Argument
Jassas' argument linking 'umum to wa'id and the Murji'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate on generality and specification is directly connected to the doctrine of wa'id (divine threat) and the Murji'a position, as explained by Abu Bakr al-Jassas.

Core Idea
Affirming generality demands affirming the threat; the Murji'a denied generality to avoid having to affirm punishment for sinners.

The theological stakes of the 'umum debate become clear in this final extract. Abu Bakr al-Jassas argues that generality is rooted in Arabic and was the view of the Salaf; only the Murji'a later rejected it to escape the implication that the verses of threat apply to all sinners. He also discusses an anecdote about Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, showing the divisions among scholars on this issue. This lesson reveals how seemingly technical usul al-fiqh questions directly shape kalam positions on God's justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read Jassas' statement on generality
Read the quote from Abu Bakr al-Jassas where he asserts that generality is from the Arabic language and the view of the Salaf, and that only the Murji'a opposed it.
Note how he links the Murji'a rejection to their desire to avoid affirming wa'id.
2
Analyze the Karkhi anecdote
Read the narration about Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi halting on generality of reports but affirming it in commands/prohibitions. Consider Jassas' dismissal of this report.
Jassas defends al-Karkhi's consistency.
3
Connect to wa'id
Reflect on how the Murji'a position on irja' leads them to deny generality, while the Salaf and the Maturidis affirm it, leading to affirming the threat for sinners.
This shows the practical consequences of the debate.
