بعينه باسمه الخاص وما هذا سبيله فلا وجه إلى القول بتخصيصه إذ التخصيص هو دليل يدل أن المخصوص غير داخل فى العموم، وذلك لن يكون إلا بدليل متصل وانعدم، وعند انعدامه بقيت الصيغة متعرية عن الدليل وهي عند تعريها دليل إرادة للعموم، فبعد ذلك إخراج بعض ما تناوله العموم يكون نسخا وإنهاء للحكم الثابت فيه إلى هذه المدة، وهذا متصور في الأوامر والنواهي فأما في الأخبار فغير يصير الإخبار كذبًا وهو مستحيل على الله تعالى، لم يزل المعتزلة يتمسكون بهذا الكلام. وكان أوائل أصحابنا يتنازعونهم في هذا الأصل أشد المنازعة، ولا يسلمون لهم أن القول بالعموم واجب الاعتقاد لما أن التكلم بلفظة العموم يراد بها الخصوص سائغ فيما بين أهل اللسان حتى كان ذلك يغلب على إرادة الحقيقة وهو العموم، وكذا اللفظ العاري عن القيد يجوز أن يكون المراد به القيد عند مشايخنا، وهم يأبون ذلك أشد الإباء ويجعلون دليل التخصيص أو القيد إذا كان متأخرا عن العام أو المطلق نسخاً لا بيانًا أن المتكلم أراد به من أول ما تكلم ما وراء ما دخل تحت القيد ودليل الخصوص، وأصحابنا كانوا يجعلون ذلك بيانًا وكانوا رحمهم الله — الفريقان جميعًا يجعلان هذا الأصل من نتائج مسألة الوعيد، وكان كل من تكلم من أوائلنا وأوائلهم في مسألة العام والمطلق يذكرون أن القول بها يؤدي إلى القول بتحقيق الوعيد أو بجواز العفو حتى إن أبا بكر الجصاص قال بعد ما استشهد لصحة قوله فبان بما وصفنا أن العموم من مفهوم لسان العرب، وأن ذلك من مذهب السلف من غير خلاف بينهم وما خالف من هذا أحد من السلف ومن بعدهم إلى أن نشأت فرقة من المرجئة ضاق عليهم المذهب في القول بالإرجاء فلجأت إلى دفع القول بالعموم رأسًا لئلا يلزمها لخصومها القول بوعيد الفساق بظاهر الآي المقتضية لذلك. هذا كله لفظه ذكره في كتابه "المُصنف في أصول الفقه" وقال أيضا في أول هذا الباب وحكى لي أبو الطيب بن شهاب عن أبي الحسن الكرخي أنه قال : إني أقف في عموم الأخبار وأقول بالعموم في الأمر والنهي، فقلت لأبي الطيب فهذا يدل على أن مذهبه كان الوقف في وعيد فساق أهل الملة، فقال : هكذا كان مذهبه، قال الجصاص وأبو الطيب هذا غير مهم عندي فيما يحكيه، وقد جالس أبا سعيد البردعي وشيوخنا القدماء ولم أسمع أن أبا الحسن الكرخي يفرق بين الخير والأمر والنهي في ذلك بل كان يقول بالعموم والإطلاق.
By its specific name, and what is on this path, there is no justification for saying it is specified, for specification is an evidence indicating that the specified is not included in the generality, and that cannot be except with a connected evidence, and it is absent; and when it is absent, the formula remains bare of evidence, and when it is bare, it is evidence of intending generality. Therefore, after that, excluding some of what the generality encompasses is abrogation and termination of the established ruling in it until that time. This is conceivable in commands and prohibitions. As for reports, it is not [conceivable] because the report becomes a lie, and that is impossible for God Almighty. The Mu'tazila have always clung to this speech. And the early ones of our companions used to dispute with them on this principle most severely, and they would not concede to them that the statement of generality is obligatory to believe, because speaking a general word intending a specific meaning is permissible among the people of the language, to the extent that this often overcomes the intended reality which is generality. Likewise, the word bare of restriction, it is permissible that the intended meaning is the restriction according to our masters, but they (the Mu'tazila) reject that most severely and make the evidence of specification or restriction, if it comes after the general or absolute, abrogation, not clarification, [claiming] that the speaker intended from the first time he spoke what is beyond the restriction and the evidence of specificity. And our companions used to consider that clarification, and they — may God have mercy on them — both groups all consider this principle to be from the results of the issue of threat (wa'id). And everyone who spoke from our early ones and their early ones regarding the issue of the general and the absolute used to mention that the statement on it leads to the statement of actualizing the threat or allowing pardon, until Abu Bakr al-Jassas said, after he adduced evidence for the correctness of his statement, 'It became clear by what we described that generality is from the concept of the language of the Arabs, and that is from the doctrine of the predecessors without disagreement among them. And no one among the predecessors or after them opposed this until a group from the Murji'a arose, [for whom] the doctrine on irja' became constrained, so they resorted to rejecting the statement on generality entirely, lest their opponents force upon them the statement of the threat of sinners by the apparent verses that necessitate that.' All this is his wording, mentioned in his book 'al-Musannaf fi Usul al-Fiqh'. And he also said at the beginning of this chapter: Abu al-Tayyib ibn Shihab narrated to me from Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi that he said: 'I stop (waqafa) regarding the generality of reports, and I affirm generality in command and prohibition.' I said to Abu al-Tayyib: 'This indicates that his doctrine was stopping on the threat of sinful people of the religion.' He said: 'That was his doctrine.' Al-Jassas said: 'Abu al-Tayyib is not important to me in what he narrates; he used to frequent Abu Sa'id al-Barda'i and our ancient masters, and I did not hear that Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi distinguished between report and command and prohibition in that; rather, he used to affirm generality and absoluteness.'

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argumentative discussion about whether general (عام) expressions in scripture can be intended to mean specific (خاص) things, and how this relates to the theological issue of divine threat (الوعيد) and pardon (العفو). The author begins by stating the Mu'tazila position: if a general statement is given with its specific name, there is no basis for saying it is specified (تخصيص) because specification requires a separate evidence; in its absence the statement remains proof of generality. Excluding some parts would then be abrogation (نسخ), not clarification (بيان), and this applies to commands and prohibitions, but not to reports (since that would entail falsehood, impossible for God). The Mu'tazila continuously rely on this reasoning. Then the author explains that the early Maturidi/Hanafi scholars (أوائل أصحابنا) strongly opposed the Mu'tazila on this principle. They did not accept that affirming generality is obligatory in belief, because it is permissible in Arabic for a general expression to intend a specific meaning, and similarly for an unrestricted word to intend a restriction. However, the Mu'tazila reject this and treat delayed specification as abrogation rather than clarification, claiming the speaker intended the restricted meaning from the outset. The Maturidi scholars, by contrast, considered such delayed evidence as clarification (بيان) of the original intention. Next, the author notes that both groups linked this issue to the problem of al-wa'id: the threat of punishment for sinners. Those who affirm generality tend to uphold the threat (i.e., that the revealed threats apply to all sinners), while those who allow specification (takhsis) leave room for pardon (عفو). He then quotes Abu Bakr al-Jassas, who argued that generality is part of Arabic usage and was the view of the Salaf, and that only the Murji'a later denied generality to avoid having to affirm the threat of punishment for sinners. Al-Jassas also reports a narration from Abu al-Tayyib about Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi: al-Karkhi allegedly said he stops (يقف) on the generality of reports (i.e., does not affirm it) but affirms it for commands and prohibitions. The passage thus presents a complex interweaving of linguistic-legal theory (usul al-fiqh) with theological doctrine (kalam), showing how seemingly neutral technical rules about textual interpretation have direct implications for beliefs about God's promises and threats.

Maturidi Position on Generality vs. Specificity
Al-Maturidi's view on 'umum and the school's later shift.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the original Maturidi position on the relationship between general and specific expressions in usul al-fiqh, and the school's later shift toward the Iraqi view.

Core Idea
The Maturidi school originally saw delayed specificity as clarification, not abrogation.

This passage from Tabsirat al-Adilla documents an important doctrinal distinction within the Maturidi school. Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and early masters held that when evidence of specificity comes after a general statement, it clarifies the intended meaning rather than abrogating the general rule. This set them apart from the Mu'tazila. The passage also describes how some later Maturidis shifted toward the Iraqi Hanafi position, a development the author notes critically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author first states al-Maturidi's view, then contrasts it with the Mu'tazila, and finally describes the change.
2
Identify the key concepts
The passage mentions 'umum, khusus, bayan, naskh. Understand the Maturidi rule that later evidence of specificity is bayan, not naskh.
3
Reflect on the school's evolution
Notice the author's tone: he disapproves of those who adopted the Iraqi view and misattributed the original position to al-Shafi'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي وغيره من مشايخنا رحمهم الله - كانوا يقولون إن العموم يذكر ويراد به الخصوص، وما يقوم من دليل الخصوص متأخر إلا دليل القيد فهو بيان المراد لا النسخ والصيغة المتعربة عن دليل الخصوص أو القيد ليست بدليل إرادة العموم والإطلاق ويعدون القول بذلك مذهب المعتزلة، وكان مشايخنا في ديارنا ينكرون على القائلين بذلك وينسبونهم إلى الاعتزال إلى أن نشأ فيهم من كان يميل في أصول الفقه إلى العراقيين من أصحابنا فأتبعهم في مسألة العموم وذكر ما تصوره عنده أنه دليل له في المسألة ونسب ما هو قول أصحابنا إلى الشافعي ولم يحم حول مسألة الوعيد، والله تعالى أعلم بعقيدته في ذلك، وقد
And Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and others of our masters — may God have mercy on them — used to say that generality is mentioned and by it is intended specificity, and whatever evidence of specificity comes later, except that it is evidence of restriction, it is clarification of the intended meaning, not abrogation. And the formula that is bare of the evidence of specificity or restriction is not evidence of intending generality and absoluteness, and they consider the statement of that to be the doctrine of the Mu'tazila. And our masters in our region used to denounce those who said that and attribute them to Mu'tazilism, until there arose among them one who inclined in usul al-fiqh toward the Iraqis from our companions, so he followed them on the issue of generality and mentioned what he imagined to be evidence for him in the issue, and he attributed what is our companions' view to al-Shafi'i, and he did not circle around the issue of threat. And God Almighty knows best concerning his belief in that, and
Source pages p. 1060

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and other senior Maturidi scholars regarding the relationship between general (عام) and specific (خاص) expressions. They affirm that a general expression may be intended to convey a specific meaning, and when evidence of specificity comes later, it is clarification (بيان) of the original intent, not abrogation (نسخ). Moreover, a statement devoid of such evidence of specificity or restriction does not automatically prove that generality or absoluteness was intended. They consider the opposing view — that general expressions must always be taken as general unless abrogated — to be the position of the Mu'tazila. The author then notes that in his region (likely Transoxiana), the early Maturidi scholars strongly denounced those who held the Mu'tazili view and labeled them as Mu'tazilis. However, a shift occurred: a later scholar among them began to incline toward the Iraqi (i.e., Hanafi-Basran or Mu'tazili-influenced) position in usul al-fiqh. He followed the Iraqis on the issue of generality, presented what he thought was evidence for that view, and misattributed the original Maturidi position to al-Shafi'i. Notably, this scholar avoided engaging with the issue of wa'id (threat). The author concludes by leaving judgment of that scholar's true belief to God. The passage thus documents an internal development within the Maturidi school, where the original position was distinct from both the Mu'tazila and later Iraqi Hanafis who adopted a view closer to the Mu'tazila on this point.

الخصوص
البيان
النسخ

العراقيون
الوعيد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Maturidi's view on generality is the same as that of the Mu'tazila.
Correction: The passage explicitly distinguishes al-Maturidi's view: delayed specificity is clarification, not abrogation, and they consider the opposing view as Mu'tazili.

Checkpoint

A) It is abrogation (naskh) of the general ruling
B) It is clarification (bayan) of the intended meaning
C) It invalidates the general statement entirely
D) It proves that the general was never meant to be general

Key Takeaway
The original Maturidi school taught that delayed evidence of specificity clarifies rather than abrogates the general statement, and they saw the opposite as Mu'tazili; later some adopted the Iraqi Hanafi view.

Abu Mansur and the Wa'id: A Defense Against Waqifism
Nasafi defends Abu Mansur against the charge of waqifism in wa'id.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Mansur al-Maturidi was mistakenly called a waqifi concerning the fate of the grave sinner, and what his actual methodological position was.

Core Idea
Abu Mansur was not a waqifi; he insisted on acting upon general evidence unless specified.

This passage clarifies a major misunderstanding about Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi's stance on the divine threat (wa'id) against major sinners. Critics accused him of suspending judgment, but Nasafi explains that Abu Mansur's position was rooted in a careful distinction between obligatory (wājib) and farḍ based on the strength of evidence. Let's examine the text to see how this defense unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without trying to analyze deeply. Let the flow of Nasafi's argument come through.
Pay attention to the initial statement about Abu Mansur and the reaction of later scholars.
2
Identify the accusation and defense
Notice how Nasafi first mentions the accusation that Abu Mansur was a waqifi. Then look for how he refutes it by explaining the real doctrine.
3
Understand the principles of umum and the wājib/farḍ distinction
Nasafi explains that acting on general (umum) and absolute wording is wājib (obligatory) when based on non-definitive evidence. Definitive evidence yields farḍ.
This is key to understanding why Abu Mansur was not suspending judgment.
4
See how specification resolves the issue
Nasafi points out that those who accept umum and allow forgiveness for the grave sinner restrict the general through specification (takhṣīṣ). This is exactly what Abu Mansur and other theologians did.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أطلق في كتابه أن الفاسق المطلق هو صاحب الكبيرة ولست أدري أقال ذلك علمًا منه بما يؤدي إليه هذا القول أم سنح له شيء فتكلم به من غير العلم بما يؤدي إليه والسرائر موكولة إلى الله تعالى وهو أعلم بالعقائد والضمائر، فاتبعه جميع المنتسبين إلى التحقيق في ذلك واقتدوا به وما اشتغل أحد منهم أنه بأي طريق يتكلم في مسألة الوعيد، وسموا الشيخ أبا منصور رحمه الله الواقفي في هذه المسألة جهلاً منهم بمذهبه إذ حقيقة مذهبه أن العمل بالعموم وبمطلق الصيغة واجب، إذ الواجب هو ما ثبت بدليل غير مقطوع به وهو دليل الظاهر، فأما ما ثبت بدليل مقطوع به فهو الفرض، ولم يرد عن أحد من متقدمي أصحابنا أنهم قالوا مطلق الأمر للوجوب والقول بالعموم واجب وهو ما ذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور، الواقفي فهو من يقف في ذلك عملا واعتقادا، ثم إن من ساعد هذا القائل بالعموم وظهر منه القول بجواز مغفرة صاحب الكبيرة يضيق عليه الأمر بطريق التخصيص كما ذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور، وجميع من ساعدنا في هذه المسألة من فحول المتكلمين كأبي الحسن الأشعري ومن تابعه وابن الراوندي والحسين وغيرهم
He stated in his book that the absolute sinner (al-fāsiq al-muṭlaq) is the one who commits a major sin (ṣāḥib al-kabīra). I do not know whether he said that knowingly of what this statement entails, or something occurred to him and he spoke without knowledge of what it entails; and secrets are entrusted to God Almighty, and He knows best beliefs and consciences. All those who claimed to be investigators followed him in that and emulated him, but none of them concerned themselves with the question: by what method does he speak about the issue of the threat (al-wa‘īd)? They called the Shaykh Abū Manṣūr – may God have mercy on him – the waqifī in this issue, out of ignorance of his doctrine. For the reality of his doctrine is that acting upon the general (al-‘umūm) and upon the absolute wording (muṭlaq al-ṣīgha) is obligatory (wājib), for the obligatory is that which is established by a non-definitive proof (dalīl ghayr maqṭū‘ bihī), which is the apparent proof (dalīl al-ẓāhir). As for that which is established by a definitive proof (dalīl maqṭū‘ bihī), it is the obligatory duty (al-farḍ). It has not been reported from any of our early companions that they said the absolute command is for obligation (wujūb), but rather that the statement of generality (‘umūm) is obligatory – and that is what Shaykh Abū Manṣūr held. The waqifī is one who suspends judgment in that, in both practice and belief. Then, one who assists this proponent of generality and from whom appears the statement that the forgiveness of the major sinner is permissible, his situation is narrowed by way of specification (al-takhṣīṣ), as held by Shaykh Abū Manṣūr, and all those who assisted us in this issue among the leading theologians (fuḥūl al-mutakallimīn), such as Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ash‘arī and those who followed him, and Ibn al-Rāwandī, and al-Ḥusayn, and others.
Source pages p. 1061

Commentary
This passage defends Abū Manṣūr al-Māturīdī against the accusation of being a waqifī (one who suspends judgment) regarding the fate of the grave sinner (fāsiq muṭlaq). The author (Abū al-Mu‘īn al-Nasafī) begins by noting that Abū Manṣūr called the absolute sinner the one who commits a major sin. Nasafī expresses uncertainty whether Abū Manṣūr said this knowingly or without full awareness of its implications, but leaves the hidden matters to God. He then states that all later scholars followed Abū Manṣūr in this, but none investigated his method. They mistakenly labeled him a waqifī out of ignorance of his actual doctrine. Nasafī explains: Abū Manṣūr's real position is that acting upon general (‘umūm) and absolute (muṭlaq) expressions is obligatory (wājib) when supported by non-definitive evidence (dalīl ẓāhirī), whereas definitive evidence yields the higher category of farḍ. The early Hanafi scholars did not hold that the absolute command indicates obligation per se; rather, they held that affirming the general term is obligatory. Thus Abū Manṣūr's view is not waqifism but adherence to the principle of working with general and specific. Then Nasafī notes that those who accept the general term and allow forgiveness for the major sinner restrict the general through specification (takhṣīṣ), as Abū Manṣūr and many prominent theologians like al-Ash‘arī, Ibn al-Rāwandī, and al-Ḥusayn did.

Key Terms
فاسق مطلق
وعيد
واقفي
عموم
تخصيص
واجب
فرض
دليل الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Mansur was a waqifi (one who suspends judgment) regarding the divine threat against major sinners.
Correction: Abu Mansur held that acting upon general and absolute wording is obligatory (wājib) based on non-definitive evidence, and he allowed specification (takhṣīṣ) to restrict the general, which is a standard uṣūlī methodology, not suspension.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the real position of Abu Mansur regarding the general threat (wa'id)?
A) He suspended judgment entirely, making him a waqifi.
B) He held that acting upon the general and absolute wording is obligatory when based on non-definitive evidence.
C) He denied the possibility of forgiveness for the major sinner.
D) He considered the threat to be definitive for every sinner.

Key Takeaway
Abu Mansur's doctrine on wa'id is consistent with the Hanafi principle of prioritizing general evidence unless specified, not a suspension of judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone’s theological position as extreme or ignorant without first understanding the principles they rely on? Will you commit to investigating the methodological foundations of a view before labeling it?
?
When you encounter general statements in scripture about punishment, do you instinctively apply them unconditionally, or do you actively seek possible specifications? How might this lesson change your approach to interpreting such texts?
?
Abu Mansur was misunderstood and mislabeled by his contemporaries. Is there a theological or practical stance you hold that others might misrepresent? Are you willing to clarify your own methodology, even if it means being misjudged?

Refuting Mu'tazila on Intercession
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila using two verses about intercession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila by reinterpreting key Quranic verses about intercession to include sinful believers.

Core Idea
Faith and good deeds ensure a believer remains pleasing to Allah, thus eligible for intercession.

The Mu'tazila argued that sinful believers cannot receive intercession because Allah only intercedes for those He is pleased with, and sinners are not pleased. They also cited a verse stating wrongdoers have no intercessor. Nasafi refutes both arguments by carefully defining who qualifies as 'pleased' and who qualifies as a 'wrongdoer' in the Quranic context. Let's see his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation of the first verse
Carefully read the part where Nasafi responds to the Mu'tazila's use of 'They do not intercede except for whom He is pleased with.' Notice how he defines 'pleased' as including any believer with faith and good deeds.
The key is that the verse does not say 'only sinless are pleased.'
2
Analyze the second verse rebuttal
Then read the refutation of 'For the wrongdoers there is no friend nor any intercessor.' Nasafi restricts this verse to disbelievers only, clarifying that a sinful believer is not an absolute wrongdoer.
The term 'wrongdoer' in its absolute sense applies only to disbelievers in this context.
3
Connect to the overall argument
Consider how the two refutations together uphold the possibility of intercession for sinful believers. The Mu'tazila's proof texts do not actually exclude them.
This is a classic example of tafsir-based theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعلقهم بقوله تعالى: وَلَا يَشْفَعُونَ إِلَّا لِمَنِ ارْتَضَى قلنا: وكل مؤمن هو ممن ارتضاه الله تعالى وما معه من الإيمان والطاعات والحسنات المرضية (عند الله) تعالى لا يخرج من كونه مرضيا عند الله تعالى. وقيل: ولا يشفعون إلا لمن ارتضى الله أن يشفعوا له، فلم زعمتم أن الله تعالى لا يرضى بشفاعة صاحب الكبيرة وفيه الخلاف، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ مَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ مِنْ حَمِيمٍ وَلَا شَفِيعٍ يُطَاعُ ) منصرف إلى الكافر إذ هو الظالم المطلق الذي لا عدل معه، فأما المؤمن الذي معه الإيمان والأعمال الصالحة فلا يسمى ظالمًا على الإطلاق
And their clinging to His statement: 'And they do not intercede except for whom He is pleased with.' We said: And every believer is among those whom Allah is pleased with, and what is with him of faith, acts of obedience, and good deeds that are pleasing (to Allah) does not remove him from being pleasing to Allah Almighty. And it was said: And they do not intercede except for whom Allah is pleased that they should intercede for. So why do you claim that Allah Almighty is not pleased with the intercession of the perpetrator of a major sin? And therein lies the dispute. And His statement: 'For the wrongdoers there is no friend nor any intercessor to be obeyed' is directed to the disbeliever, for he is the absolute wrongdoer who has no justice with him. As for the believer who has faith and righteous deeds, he is not called a wrongdoer absolutely.
Source pages p. 1075

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila using two Quranic verses. It begins by addressing their argument from the verse in Surah al-Anbiya' (21:28) that intercession is only for those Allah is pleased with. The author responds that every believer who has faith and good deeds is among those Allah is pleased with, so the verse does not exclude sinful believers. Then he addresses another verse from Surah Ghafir (40:18) which states that wrongdoers have no intercessor. He argues that this refers specifically to disbelievers, who are absolute wrongdoers with no justice. A believer who commits sins but has faith and good deeds is not called a wrongdoer absolutely, so the verse does not apply to him. Thus intercession for sinful believers is not ruled out. The argument follows a logical order: first refuting the restriction of 'pleased' to sinless, then refuting the exclusion of sinful believers as 'wrongdoers'.

Key Terms
شفاعة
ارتضى
ظالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that the Quranic verses about intercession being only for those Allah is pleased with, and wrongdoers having no intercessor, prove that sinful believers cannot receive intercession.
Correction: Nasafi shows that 'pleased' includes any believer with faith and good deeds, and 'wrongdoers' in the absolute sense refers to disbelievers, not sinful believers.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, to whom does the verse 'مَا لِلظَّالِمِينَ مِنْ حَمِيمٍ وَلَا شَفِيعٍ يُطَاعُ' refer?
A) All wrongdoers including sinful believers
B) Only disbelievers
C) Only Muslims
D) Everyone

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila objections to intercession for sinful believers by showing that the Quranic verses do not exclude them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you still consider yourself among those with whom Allah is pleased? Why does your heart struggle to accept that your faith and good deeds keep you in His pleasure, even when you fall? Commit to reciting the verse 'They intercede not except for whom He is pleased with' with the conviction that you are included, and see if it changes your hope.
?
Have you ever, like the Mu'tazila, limited Allah's mercy by assuming that your sins disqualify you from His intercession? What would change in your relationship with Allah if you truly believed that your iman and righteous acts make you always eligible for His pleasure?
?
In this passage, Nasafi argues that a believer is not called a 'wrongdoer' absolutely. Do you define yourself by your sins or by your faith? When you sin, do you identify more as a 'sinner' or as a 'believer who sinned'? How would this shift in identity affect your turning to Allah in repentance and hope for intercession?

Refutation of Mu'tazila on Angels Asking Paradise for Its People
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila claim that angels asking for paradise is absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that angels asking for paradise is absurd, using a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila premise that paradise is obligatory leads to absurd conclusions about angelic acts.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi continues his refutation of the Mu'tazila by examining a specific claim they make about the angels. The Mu'tazila argue that it would be absurd for angels to ask for Paradise for its people, because heaven is already obligatory. Nasafi turns this argument on its head, showing that their own assumption leads to an even greater absurdity. Let's read his rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the emotional tone—Nasafi is refuting a serious claim.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila premise
The Mu'tazila believe that entering Paradise is an obligatory right for its people. This is the key assumption Nasafi will work with.
This premise is central to their doctrine of justice.
3
Follow Nasafi's logic
Nasafi present two possibilities for the angels' request given the Mu'tazila premise: either they fear Allah will be unjust, or they engage in frivolity. Both are impossible for the best of creation.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the method
Ask yourself: Why does Nasafi start with the Mu'tazila's own premise? How does this strengthen his refutation?
He is not imposing an external argument but showing internal inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن الملائكة يسألون الجنة لأهل الجنة فهذا منه على مذهبه دعوى العبث على خيرة خلق الله تعالى في يوم الجمع الأكبر؛ لأن إدخال أهل الجنة (الجنة واجب) عندهم وحق مستحق لأهل ذلك وعلى هذا كان اشتغال الملائكة بسؤال ذلك إما لأنهم خافوا أن يظلم الله تعالى ويمنع أهلها من دخولها ويمنعهم حقهم الواجب عليهم، وإما إن اشتغلوا بالعبث فيقولون أعطهم ما أعطيتهم واغفر لمن غفرت له
And what they claim that the angels ask for Paradise for the people of Paradise — this, according to his (the Mu'tazilite's) doctrine, is an allegation of frivolity against the best of Allah's creation on the Day of the Great Assembly. Because entering Paradise (for the people of Paradise) is obligatory according to them and an entitled right for its people. And upon this, the angels' occupation with asking for that is either because they feared that Allah would be unjust and prevent its people from entering and deprive them of their due right, or they engaged in frivolity by saying 'Give them what You have given them and forgive whom You have forgiven.'
Source pages p. 1075

Commentary
This passage refutes a Mu'tazila position on the intercession of angels. The author points out that if, as the Mu'tazila claim, paradise is an obligatory right for its people, then the angels asking for it would be either out of fear that Allah would be unjust (which is impossible) or mere frivolity. Both are absurd and unbecoming of the best of creation. Thus the Mu'tazila premise leads to an impossible conclusion. This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Key Terms
عبث
وجوب
ظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila view of divine justice requires that reward be obligatory, but this leads to absurd consequences for angelic acts.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazila premise actually makes the angels' request either imply divine injustice or frivolity, both of which are unacceptable.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi say is the consequence of the Mu'tazila belief that paradise is obligatory?
A) Angels would be rewarded
B) Angels would have nothing to ask for
C) Angels' asking would be either fear of injustice or frivolity
D) Angels would not exist

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila position that paradise is an obligatory right leads to an unacceptable conclusion about angelic actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make dua for something you believe Allah has already promised or obligated Himself to give, do you sometimes feel it's unnecessary? How does this lesson challenge that feeling, and what does it say about your view of Allah's mercy?
?
If you truly believed that your good deeds 'obligate' Allah to reward you, how would that affect your humility and reliance on His grace? Can you identify areas in your life where you act as if Allah 'owes' you something?
?
The angels ask for Paradise for its people despite it being already decreed. What does that teach you about the nature of worship and supplication in the presence of divine decree? Will you now approach your duas with a different attitude, knowing that even angels ask for what is already given?

Refutation of Mu'tazila on Forgiveness for Those Who Repent
Nasafi interprets verse as repentance from shirk, not from all sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the verse 'forgive those who repent' refers specifically to repentance from shirk, not from all sins, refuting the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
The verse's forgiveness is for those who repent from shirk, not from every sin.

This lesson examines a key verse used by the Mu'tazila to support their doctrine that God only forgives those who repent from all sins. Imam Nasafi argues that the repentance mentioned is specifically from shirk, as indicated by the phrase 'and follow Your way.' He shows that the verse does not restrict forgiveness to those who repent from all major sins but rather refers to those who turn from disbelief while still having other sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Pay attention to how Nasafi quotes the verse and immediately gives his interpretation.
2
Identify Nasafi's interpretation and evidence
Find where Nasafi states that the repentance is from shirk and what evidence he provides from the verse itself.
Look for the phrase 'أي اغفر للذين تابوا عن الشرك' and the evidence from 'واتبعوا سبيلك'.
3
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
Notice how Nasafi concludes that interpreting the verse as the Mu'tazila do is impossible. Consider why he mentions that the people still have sins and major offenses.
This shows that forgiveness is not conditional on repentance from all sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَأَغْفِرْ لِلَّذِينَ تَابُوا وَاتَّبَعُوا سَبِيلَكَ ) أي اغفر للذين تابوا عن الشرك بدليل قوله : واتبعوا سبيلك أي تابوا عن الكفر ولهم ذنوب اقترفوها وكبائر ارتكبوها في حالة الإيمان، فأما الصرف إلى ما صرفته المعتزلة فممتنع لما ذكرت
And His statement: 'So forgive those who repent and follow Your way' — i.e., forgive those who repent from shirk, as evidenced by His saying 'and follow Your way' — i.e., they repented from disbelief, and they have sins they committed and major offenses they perpetrated in the state of faith. As for turning it to what the Mu'tazila turned it to, it is impossible for what I mentioned.
Source pages p. 1075

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation of Surah Ghafir (40:7) where angels ask forgiveness for those who repent and follow Allah's way. The author argues that the repentance mentioned here is specifically from shirk (polytheism), not from all sins. The phrase 'follow Your way' indicates they repented from disbelief. These people still have sins and major offenses that they committed while being believers. Therefore the verse does not prove that God only forgives those who repent from all sins; rather it shows forgiveness for those who repent from shirk while still having other sins. Hence Mu'tazila's restriction of forgiveness to those who repent from all major sins is invalid.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse restricts forgiveness only to those who repent from all sins.
Correction: The verse refers specifically to repentance from shirk, as shown by the context, and does not require repentance from every sin for forgiveness.

Checkpoint

B) Major sins
C) Shirk
D) All sins

Key Takeaway
Nasafi argues that the repentance in the verse is specifically from shirk, not from all sins, thereby refuting the Mu'tazila interpretation that would restrict God's forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Nasafi says that believers still commit major offenses even in a state of faith—what major sin are you currently comfortable with, thinking that God will forgive it anyway without you needing to repent?
?
If you were to die tonight, would you be relying on your own repentance from all major sins, or on God's mercy that forgives believers even with their shortcomings?

Refutation of Mu'tazila on Praying for Enemies
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila claim that praying for God's enemies is impermissible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi rejects the Mu'tazila claim that it is impermissible to pray for God's enemies, and how he exposes its corrupt foundation.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's premise that believers are God's enemies is fundamentally corrupt.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi continues his refutation of Mu'tazila theology by addressing their claim that righteous believers cannot pray for God's enemies. He argues that this claim rests on a false assumption: that believers who commit major sins are enemies of God. By questioning this assumption, Nasafi dismantles the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the argument. Pay attention to the phrase 'على أصله الفاسد' (built upon his corrupt foundation).
This phrase is key to the refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The Mu'tazila argue that it is not permissible for the righteous or the Prophet to pray for God's enemies or to ask Allah to admit them to Paradise. Understand exactly what they are prohibiting.
3
Understand Nasafi's refutation
Nasafi dismisses the argument as based on a corrupt foundation. He asks a rhetorical question: who would accept that a believer with countless good deeds is an enemy of God? This challenges the premise that the Mu'tazila assume.
The question forces a reconsideration of who qualifies as an enemy of God.
4
Connect to the previous discussion
Nasafi mentions that the details of this dispute have already been covered in a preceding issue. This shows continuity in the text and that the argument is part of a larger theological debate.
Review the previous lesson if available to see the broader context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قال إنه لا يجوز أن يقول الأخيار اللهم اجعل أعداءك وأهل نارك أهل جنتك وافعل خلاف ما قلت، ولا يجوز أن يجيب النبي عليه السلام من أبغضه الله ويوالي من عاداه الله هذا كله كلام بناه على أصله الفاسد، ومن الذي يسلم له أن المؤمن الذي زجى عمره في طاعة الله تعالى ومعه من الأعمال الصالحة ما لا يحصى يكون عدوا لله تعالى أو مبغضًا له، وفصل الخلف فيما قال قد مر الكلام فيه في المسألة المتقدمة
And what he said that it is not permissible for the righteous to say: 'O Allah, make Your enemies and the people of Your Fire the people of Your Paradise and do contrary to what You have said', and it is not permissible for the Prophet, peace be upon him, to answer whom Allah hates and to befriend whom Allah opposes. All this speech is built upon his corrupt foundation. And who would concede to him that the believer who has spent his life in obedience to Allah Almighty and has with him countless righteous deeds is an enemy of Allah or hated by Him? And the detail of the dispute in what he said has already been discussed in the preceding issue.

This passage refutes a Mu'tazila argument that it is impermissible for righteous people or the Prophet to pray for God's enemies or to ask God to admit His enemies to Paradise. He questions: who would accept that a believer who has spent his life in obedience and has countless good deeds is an enemy of God? Thus the Mu'tazila premise is false. The matter has already been addressed in a previous discussion.

Why Desiring Intercession Does Not Contradict Detesting Sin
Nasafi shows desiring intercession does not contradict detesting sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that desiring intercession is compatible with detesting sin, through the analogy of desiring forgiveness.

Core Idea
Desiring intercession is like desiring forgiveness — it does not mean approving sin.

The Mu'tazila argued that Muslims cannot both desire intercession (which implies sin) and detest sin. Nasafi responds by drawing a parallel with forgiveness: Muslims desire forgiveness but also detest committing sins. This lesson unpacks his refutation and shows why the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: an objection followed by a response.
The passage is concise; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
The objection claims that desiring intercession and detesting sin are contradictory. Find the exact wording in the text.
The objection is introduced by 'وقولهم'.
3
Analyze the author's response
Nasafi uses an analogy: Muslims desire forgiveness but detest sin. He then applies the same logic to intercession. Notice how he specifies the conditions for desiring intercession.
The analogy shows that desiring mercy after sin is not the same as desiring sin.
4
Reflect on the verification
The passage ends by stating that the desire for intercession cannot be interpreted otherwise. Think about why this closes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : المسلمون يرغبون في الشفاعة ويزهدون في أن يكونوا فساقًا. قلنا : والمسلمون يرغبون في المغفرة ويزهدون في أن يكونوا مرتكبي صغائر ، ثم نقول يرغبون في الشفاعة لو وجدت منه معصية فيما يستأنف من الوقت أو سبقت فيما مضى من الوقت ويتعوذون بالله تعالى من الفسق والمعصية كما قلتم أنتم في المغفرة. يحققه : أن رغبتهم في الشفاعة لا يمكن حملها على غير هذا الوجه فلا حاجة
And their statement: 'Muslims desire intercession and detest being sinful.' We said: And Muslims desire forgiveness and detest being perpetrators of minor sins. Then we say: They desire intercession if some sin occurs from him in the future or preceded in the past, and they seek refuge with Allah from sin and disobedience, just as you say regarding forgiveness. The verification of this is that their desire for intercession cannot be interpreted in any other way, so there is no need.
Source pages p. 1076

Commentary
This passage refutes a Mu'tazila objection that if Muslims desire intercession, it means they condone sin, but they also detest being sinful — an alleged contradiction. The author responds by analogy: Muslims desire forgiveness but also detest committing minor sins. Similarly, the desire for intercession is for sins that may occur in the future or have occurred in the past, while simultaneously seeking refuge from sin. Thus there is no contradiction. The desire for intercession is not a desire to sin, but a desire for mercy after sin, just as one desires forgiveness without approving the sin. The argument ends by stating that this interpretation is the only possible one, so the Mu'tazila point is unnecessary.

Key Terms
رغبة
زهد
شفاعة
مغفرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Desiring intercession means approving of sin or wanting to sin.
Correction: Desiring intercession is a desire for God's mercy after sin occurs, not a desire for sin itself. One can detest sin while still hoping for forgiveness or intercession.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi respond to the claim that desiring intercession contradicts detesting sin?
A) He denies that Muslims desire intercession
B) He says desire for intercession is only for future sins
C) He compares it to desiring forgiveness while detesting sin
D) He says it is a matter of faith

Key Takeaway
Desiring intercession is compatible with detesting sin because it is a desire for mercy after sin, not a desire for sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you honestly detest sin in your heart while still hoping for Allah's mercy, or do you sometimes rely on the idea of intercession as a license to sin without genuine regret?
?
When you commit a sin, do you immediately seek refuge with Allah and feel aversion to your action, or do you quietly assume intercession will cover it without any personal change?
?
How often do you check your heart's attitude: are you sincerely seeking to avoid sin, or are you complacent, hoping for intercession later while not actively detesting the sin now?

The Objection of Asking for Foolishness Refuted
Nasafi dismisses the first objection as identical to a prior refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that prophets would be asking for foolishness if intercession were not permissible is invalid.

Core Idea
Prophets never ask Allah to abandon wisdom.

The first objection against the possibility of intercession claims that if intercession were not granted, prophets would be asking God to act foolishly. Nasafi dismisses this as identical to a prior refutation, showing its absurdity. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic text and translation that presents the objection from the opponents.
Notice how the objection equates non-granting with foolishness.
2
Identify the refutation
See how Nasafi dismisses the objection as a repetition of an earlier argument.
The author considers it already refuted.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why it is impossible for a prophet to ask for something foolish.
This connects to the doctrine of prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقربين يسألون من الله تعالى ما هذا سبيله فهو كافر بالله تعالى، ولو كان لا يجوز إعطاؤه لكانوا بشفاعتهم طالبين أن يترك الباري عز وجل الحكمة ويأتي بما هو سفه، وهذا مثل الأول، وما قالوا في مسألة الحلف بالطلاق أن يفعل فعلاً يستحق به الشفاعة. قلنا: إن أردت بذلك أن يفعل فعلاً يجب على الأنبياء والرسل عليهم السلام أن يشفعوه فهذا لغو من الكلام ويصير كمن حلف أن يخلق الأجسام ويجمع بين الضدين لأن استيجاب حق على الله تعالى وعلى رسوله تصنع من الخلق غير متصور، وإن أراد أن يصير بذلك أهلاً فنأمره أن يعتقد مذهب أهل السنة، ويلعن أهل الأهواء ويتبرأ منهم ويناصب المعتزلة عداوة ويلعنهم لعنا كبيرا ليصير بذلك أهلاً لنيل الشفاعة لو بدرت منه خطيئة أو حصلت منه معصية. ثم نقول لهم : من حلف أن يفعل ما يصير به أهلاً للمغفرة فماذا تأمرونه به أتأمرونه بالطاعة ولا حاجة إلى المغفرة معها ؟ أم بالمعصية فتكفرون حينئذ؟ فمهما أجابوا من شيء فهو لهم جواب.
The close [to God] ask God for something that is like this, so he is a disbeliever in God, and if it were not permissible to give it, then they would, by their intercession, be asking that the Creator (mighty and majestic) abandon wisdom and do foolishness, and this is like the first [objection]. And what they said regarding the issue of swearing by divorce: that he does an act by which he becomes deserving of intercession. We said: If you mean by that that he does an act that obligates the prophets and messengers (peace be upon them) to intercede for him, then this is nonsense of speech, and he becomes like one who swore to create bodies and combine opposites, because obligating a right over God and over His messenger is a pretense from the creation that is inconceivable. And if he intends by that to become worthy, then we command him to believe in the doctrine of the People of the Sunna (Ahl al-Sunna), and curse the people of desires, and dissociate from them, and show enmity to the Mu'tazila, and curse them greatly, so that he becomes thereby worthy of attaining intercession, if a sin foregoes him or a disobedience occurs from him. Then we say to them: Whoever swears that he will do something by which he becomes worthy of forgiveness, what do you command him to do? Do you command him to obey, but there is no need for forgiveness with it? Or [do you command him] to disobey, and then you disbelieve him? Whatever they answer is an answer for them.
Source pages p. 1078

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections concerning intercession (shafa'ah). The first objection states that if intercession were not permissible, the prophets would be asking God to abandon wisdom and do something foolish—which is impossible. The author dismisses this as identical to a prior refutation. The second objection deals with a person who swears by divorce that he will perform an act that makes him deserving of intercession. The author presents a detailed response, first explaining that if such an oath is meant to obligate the prophets to intercede, it is impossible because no human can impose duties on Allah or His messengers. If instead it means to become worthy of intercession, then the person should adopt the beliefs and practices of Ahl al-Sunna, dissociate from deviant sects, and show enmity to the Mu'tazila. The author then raises a further argument: if someone swears to become worthy of forgiveness, what would the objectors advise—obedience (which needs no forgiveness) or disobedience (which they would condemn)? This is a rhetorical trap, as any answer will refute their objection.

Key Terms
شفاعة (intercession)
سفه (foolishness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prophets could ask God for something foolish if intercession is not granted.
Correction: Prophets are infallible and only ask what is consistent with divine wisdom; the objection fails.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi respond to the objection that prophets would be asking for foolishness?
A) He provides a detailed proof.
B) He dismisses it as identical to a prior refutation.
C) He agrees but offers a compromise.
D) He ignores it.

Key Takeaway
The claim that prophets would ask for foolishness is not a valid objection because it rests on a false premise already refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you ever guilty of asking Allah for something that, if granted, would require Him to compromise His wisdom? How does this passage challenge you to purify your requests?
?
When you hear an objection to a core belief, do you quickly label it as already refuted, or do you earnestly seek to understand the refutation? How can you cultivate intellectual humility before Allah?

The Oath for Intercession Refuted
Nasafi refutes the oath for intercession by distinguishing two meanings and ending with a rhetorical trap.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why swearing by divorce to obligate intercession is invalid and how the author uses a rhetorical trap to refute the objectors.

Core Idea
No human can obligate Allah or His messengers through an oath.

The second objection involves a person who swears by divorce to perform an act that makes him deserving of intercession. Nasafi dissects this into two possible meanings, refuting both, and then presents a rhetorical question that traps the opponents. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage about the oath by divorce and the two interpretations Nasafi considers.
The objector assumes intercession can be earned like a right.
2
Analyze the first interpretation
If the oath means to obligate the prophets to intercede, it is impossible.
No human can impose duties on Allah or His messengers.
3
Analyze the second interpretation
If it means to become worthy, then the person should follow Ahl al-Sunna and oppose the Mu'tazila.
This shows the proper path to earn intercession.
4
Examine the rhetorical trap
Nasafi asks: if someone swears to become worthy of forgiveness, what would you advise?
Any answer refutes the objector's position.

Iman: Heart and Tongue Only – A Sectarian View
Nasafi enumerates the view that iman is only heart and tongue and notes internal disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view that iman consists only of heart and tongue, along with the internal debates regarding its precise nature.

Core Idea
Iman is a matter of heart and tongue alone, but what exactly is in the heart?

In this passage, Nasafi presents the opinion that iman is confined to the heart and tongue, excluding bodily actions. He attributes this view to several sects and even many Hanafis, but notes that they disagreed among themselves: some said the heart's element is ma'rifa (knowledge), others said tasdiq (affirmation). This lesson will examine their positions and the nuances within this camp.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words guide your understanding.
Notice the groups mentioned: Shammariyya, Najjariyya, Ghaylaniyya, and companions of Abu Hanifa.
2
Identify the main claim and its proponents
The claim is that iman is only by heart and tongue, not other limbs. Identify the groups that hold this view.
This is a key distinction from the view that includes actions.
3
Understand the internal disagreement
Even among those who share this view, there is a split: some say the heart's component is ma'rifa (knowledge), others say tasdiq (affirmation). Find this in the text.
Bishr al-Marisi and Ibn al-Rawandi both held tasdiq.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this internal diversity matters for understanding early Islamic theology. It shows that the definition of iman was a live issue with multiple interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الناس من زعم أن الإيمان يكون بالقلب واللسان دون غيرهما من الجوارح وإليه يذهب الشمرية والنجارية والغيلانية وحكى هذا عن كثير من أصحاب أبي حنيفة، غير أن هؤلاء اختلفوا فيما بينهم منهم من جعل بالقلب المعرفة ومنهم من جعل ذلك التصديق. وكان بشر بن غياث المريسي يقول : إن الإيمان هو التصديق في اللغة وما ليس بتصديق فليس بإيمان إلا أن التصديق يكون بالقلب واللسان جميعًا، وإلى هذا القول ذهب ابن الراوندي.
And among people, some claim that iman takes place with the heart and the tongue, not with other limbs. This is the view of the Shammariyya, the Najjariyya, and the Ghaylaniyya, and it is reported from many of the companions of Abu Hanifa. However, these scholars differed among themselves: some of them considered it ['that which is with the heart'] to be ma'rifa, and some of them considered it to be tasdiq. And Bishr ibn Ghiyath al-Marisi used to say: 'Iman in language is tasdiq, and whatever is not tasdiq is not iman, but tasdiq occurs with both heart and tongue.' And Ibn al-Rawandi held this same view.
Source pages p. 1081

Commentary
This passage presents the second major view on iman, which restricts it to the heart and tongue, excluding bodily actions. Nasafi attributes this to the Shammariyya, Najjariyya, and Ghaylaniyya sects, and also to many followers of Abu Hanifa. However, even within this camp there is an internal disagreement: some define the heart's component as ma'rifa (knowledge), while others define it as tasdiq (affirmation). Nasafi then singles out Bishr al-Marisi, who argues that iman is essentially tasdiq (linguistic meaning), and that tasdiq must involve both heart and tongue. Ibn al-Rawandi agreed with this. Thus, while all these groups exclude actions from the definition of iman, they still debate the precise nature of the inner element.

Key Terms
القلب (the heart)
اللسان (the tongue)
المعرفة (ma'rifa)
التصديق (tasdiq)
الشمرية (Shammariyya)
النجارية (Najjariyya)
الغيلانية (Ghaylaniyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All those who say iman is confined to heart and tongue agree on its exact nature.
Correction: They actually disagreed: some said the heart's role is ma'rifa, others said tasdiq.

Checkpoint
What was the main point of disagreement among those who said iman is only in heart and tongue?
A) Whether iman is in the heart or the tongue
B) Whether the heart's component is ma'rifa or tasdiq
C) Whether actions are part of iman
D) Whether iman increases or decreases

Key Takeaway
The view that iman is only heart and tongue was held by several groups, but they differed on whether the heart's element is knowledge or affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I believe,' do you truly affirm with your heart, or is it just a lip service? The scholars in this passage debated the inner reality of iman. How honestly have you examined your own inner state? Will you set aside time today to ask Allah to show you the true state of your heart?
?
If iman is only heart and tongue, what excuse do you have for neglecting actions that your limbs could perform? Even if this sectarian view were correct, does your life reflect genuine inner conviction? If not, what will you change starting today?
?
Which group do you find yourself closer to: those who define the heart's element as knowledge (ma'rifa) or as affirmation (tasdiq)? And does your answer change how you evaluate your own iman? Are you content with mere knowledge without affirmation, or affirmation without action? What step will you take to align your belief with your practice?

Iman is Tongue Only: Three Views Compared
Nasafi presents and subdivides the view that iman is solely verbal confession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of positions that define iman solely as verbal confession, and the key differences between the views of al-Raqqashi, Ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, and the Karramiyya.

Core Idea
Even within the 'tongue only' camp, scholars disagreed on the role of inner belief.

The view that iman is only verbal confession is one of the major definitions, but it is not monolithic. Nasafi presents this view and then subdivides it into three distinct positions held by al-Raqqashi, 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, and the Karramiyya. Each has a different understanding of whether inner knowledge or affirmation is required. Let's read the passage and see how they differ.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the main claim is shared, but the three groups differ in details.
2
Identify the three positions
Find the three sub-views: al-Raqqashi, Ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, and the Karramiyya. For each, note what they require alongside confession.
Al-Raqqashi requires necessary knowledge; Ibn Sa'id requires knowledge and affirmation; Karramiyya require nothing.
3
Compare the requirements for inner belief
Ask: For each view, is inner knowledge or affirmation a condition for iman? How does each view treat the relationship between confession and inner belief?
The Karramiyya go furthest: even inner kufr does not negate iman if confession is made.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what each position implies about the nature of iman: is it an act of the heart, the tongue, or both? Which seems more plausible to you, and why?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الناس من يقول إن الإيمان يكون باللسان فحسب، وإليه كان يذهب الرقاشي وعبد الله بن سعيد القطان والكرامية غير أنهم اختلفوا فيما بينهم، فزعم الرقاشي أن الإقرار يكون إيمانًا بشرط وجود المعرفة بالقلب، والمعرفة عنده ضرورية فهي توجد لا محالة، ولا تكون إيمانًا لأنه اسم لفعل مكتسب لا لما هو ضرروي ولكن عند وجود هذا الضروري يكون الإقرار إيمانا، وكذا عبد الله بن سعيد القطان كان يقول الإيمان هو الإقرار إذا كان مقرونا بالمعرفة والتصديق ولا يكون القول عنده إيمانا بلا معرفة وتصديق، وإذا اقترن القول بهما كان القول هو الإيمان لا هما. وأما الكرامية فإنهم يزعمون أن الإيمان هو الإقرار المجرد وليس من شرط كونه إيمانا وجود التصديق والمعرفة، ويزعمون أن من اعتقد الكفر بقلبه وأقر بلسانه بالصانع وبالكتب والرسل وغير ذلك من أركان الإيمان كان مؤمنًا حقًا
And among people, some say that iman takes place by the tongue alone. This was the view of al-Raqqashi, 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, and the Karramiyya, but they differed among themselves. Al-Raqqashi claimed that confession (iqrar) becomes iman on condition that knowledge (ma'rifa) exists in the heart. For him, knowledge is necessary (daruri) and inevitably exists, but it is not itself iman, because iman is a name for an acquired action, not for something necessary. But when this necessary thing exists, the confession becomes iman. Similarly, 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan used to say: iman is the confession when it is accompanied by knowledge and affirmation (tasdiq). In his view, the statement (qawl) without knowledge and affirmation is not iman. When the statement is joined with them, the statement itself is iman, not the other two. As for the Karramiyya, they claim that iman is mere confession (al-iqrar al-mujarrad) and it is not a condition for it to be iman that affirmation (tasdiq) or knowledge (ma'rifa) exist. They claim that if a person inwardly believes in unbelief (kufr) but outwardly confesses with his tongue the Creator, the Books, the Messengers, and the other pillars of iman, he is a true believer.
Source pages p. 1082

Commentary
This passage presents the third major view: iman consists solely of verbal confession. Nasafi identifies three groups holding this view: al-Raqqashi, 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan, and the Karramiyya. However, they differ significantly. Al-Raqqashi requires inner knowledge (ma'rifa) as a necessary prerequisite: it is 'daruri' (innate/necessary) and always present, so confession becomes iman when that knowledge exists. But the knowledge itself is not iman because iman is an acquired (muktasab) act. 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Qattan also requires both knowledge and affirmation (tasdiq) alongside the confession, but he insists that only the spoken confession is called iman, not the inner components. The Karramiyya take the most extreme position: iman is mere confession (al-iqrar al-mujarrad) with no requirement of inner belief. They go so far as to say that if a person internally believes in kufr but verbally professes faith in God, scriptures, and messengers, he is a true believer. This view sharply distinguishes iman from inner conviction.

Key Terms
الإقرار (iqrar)
اللسان (the tongue)
المعرفة (ma'rifa)
التصديق (tasdiq)
الضروري (daruri)
الكرامية (Karramiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All those who say 'iman is by the tongue' mean the same thing: mere verbal confession without any inner requirement.
Correction: The passage shows three distinct positions: al-Raqqashi requires inner knowledge, Ibn Sa'id requires both knowledge and affirmation, and only the Karramiyya hold that mere confession suffices even with inner disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does al-Raqqashi require for confession to be considered iman?
A) Mere verbal confession without any inner condition
B) The presence of necessary knowledge (ma'rifa) in the heart
C) Both knowledge and affirmation (tasdiq) alongside confession
D) Performance of all religious duties

Key Takeaway
The 'tongue only' definition of iman encompasses a spectrum from requiring inner knowledge (al-Raqqashi) to requiring both knowledge and affirmation (Ibn Sa'id) to requiring nothing but the spoken word (Karramiyya).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you met someone who perfectly recites the shahada but lives a life of open rebellion against Allah, how does this lesson force you to question the state of your own faith? What would it take for your tongue to be a true reflection of your heart, or do you accept a gap between them?
?
The Karramiyya claim that a person who inwardly disbelieves but verbally professes is a true believer. Does that view make you more or less concerned about the sincerity of your own worship? In what specific area of your life are you most tempted to rely on outward show rather than inner conviction?
?
Al-Raqqashi taught that knowledge of God is necessary and always present, but it only becomes iman when confessed. Is your own knowledge of God something that compels you to speak, or do you keep it buried? What is one truth about Allah you know but have not yet let your tongue declare openly?

Refutation of Two Erroneous Views on Imamate
Refutes Rawafid and Karamiyya on multiple imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two erroneous positions on multiple imams held by the Rawafid and Karamiyya, and why they are invalid according to the established rule of single imamate.

Core Idea
Only one legitimate imam can exist at a time.

This passage applies the previously established principles of imamate to refute two specific sectarian claims. The Rawafid assert a speaking and a silent imam coexisting, while the Karamiyya claim that both Ali and Muawiya were valid imams simultaneously. The author declares both views invalid, reinforcing the rule that imamate is a single office.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the concise style and the two refutations presented.
The passage is a concluding summary.
2
Identify the two groups and their claims
The Rawafid claim two imams exist together—one speaking (active) and one silent (hidden). The Karamiyya claim Ali and Muawiya were both imams. Understand each claim.
3
Understand why these are invalid
Based on earlier rules, imamate cannot be shared. The speaking/silent distinction does not legitimize dual imamate, and historical conflict does not create co-imamate. The author declares both invalid.
This reinforces the unity of leadership in Islam.

And by pausing upon this summary, the invalidity of the Rawafid's statement is known—that there exist two imams at one time, one speaking and the other silent—and the invalidity of the Karamiyya's statement that 'Ali and Mu'awiya were both imams.

The Rawafid claim that there can be two imams simultaneously, one speaking (active) and one silent (hidden). The Karamiyya claim that 'Ali and Mu'awiya were both imams at the same time. Both are invalid according to the rule that only one imam is permissible at a time.

The Ruling on Multiple Imams and Refutation of Casting Lots
The author argues against multiple imams and refutes the lot method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic political theology rejects multiple simultaneous imams and why casting lots is not a valid method for resolving conflicting claims.

Core Idea
Multiple imams cause disunity; casting lots does not establish rights.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi addresses two key issues in the doctrine of imamate: the impermissibility of having more than one imam at a time, and the invalidity of using lots to settle disputes. The author builds his argument on consensus, political unity, and a critical examination of a minority opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the full argument. Notice the structure: first the rejection of multiple imams, then the exception of a sea barrier, then the ruling on conflicting contracts, and finally the refutation of casting lots.
The passage uses analogical reasoning (like two guardians marrying a minor) to illustrate simultaneous contracts.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Identify the argument against multiple imams (based on consensus and consequences of disunity) and the argument against casting lots (based on its purpose being only to soothe hearts, not to establish rights).
The author calls the lot method 'corrupt' because it relies on chance from an ignorant source.
3
Reflect on the exception for the sea barrier
Consider why the author makes an exception when a sea barrier prevents mutual support. This shows that the prohibition against multiple imams is not absolute but tied to the ability to maintain unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الزيادة على ذلك لجاز الأربعة والعشرة والمائة فيؤدي إلى أن ينصب في كل قرية ومحلة وسكة إمام على حدة، وذا باطل لما فيه من مخالفة إجماع الصحابة والسعي في تفرقة كلمة (۱) المسلمين (۲) ، وتحصيل سبب الفشل والجبن والتقاعد عن مقاومة أهل الحرب، لأن البعض من الأئمة لا يطيع البعض ولا ينقاد لأوامره ولا يصغي إلى رأيه، بل كل يستبد بأموره ويتبع رأي نفسه، وفي التفرق والتنازع وانحلال القوى وانفساخ العزائم، وفي الاجتماع والتعاضد والتناصر القوة والشركة وشدة الجانب وخشونة المس اللهم إلا أن يكون بين البلدين بحر حاجز لا يمكن للبعض القيام بنصرة البعض بل انقطع بسبب الحاجز التناصر والتعاون، فحينئذ لا بأس بذلك. ثم لو كان عقد لواحد صالح للأمر ثم عقد لآخر بعده، فالثاني باغ غير مفترض يجب خلعه، وإن (۳) أبى إلا التمادي في اللجاج فيقاتل كما هو الحكم في أهل البغي، ولو عقد من هو أهل لعقد الإمامة لرجل (٤) صالح لها وقوم آخر لرجل آخر ووقع العقدان معًا تعارضا فاحتيج إلى استئناف عقد لأحدهما أو يعقد لغيرهما ممن يصلح للإمامة، كوليين زوجا صغيرة ووقعا العقدين معا()، وحكى عن أبي العباس القلانسي أنه كان يقول : يقرع بينهما فمن خرجت قرعته فهو إمام، وإليه ذهب أبو القاسم الكعبي ذكره في كتاب عيون المسائل وهذا فاسد إذ هو تحكيم من ظهر جهله وهو فاسد، وكذا القرعة عندنا مشروعة لتطييب القلوب دون إثبات الحقوق، والله الموفق.
If more than that were allowed, then four, ten, and a hundred would be permissible, leading to the appointment of an imam in every village, district, and alley separately. This is invalid because it contradicts the consensus of the Companions, seeks to divide the word of the Muslims, and causes failure, cowardice, and reluctance to resist the people of war, because some imams would not obey others, nor submit to their commands, nor listen to their opinion; rather each would act independently and follow his own opinion. Division, conflict, disintegration of strength, and dissolution of resolve result, whereas unity, mutual support, and assistance bring strength, partnership, strong side, and harshness of touch. Unless there is a sea barrier between the two lands that prevents some from supporting others, and mutual support and cooperation are cut off due to the barrier, then there is no harm in that. Then, if a contract is made for one person suitable for the office, then another contract is made for another after him, the second is a transgressor (baghi) not to be obeyed and must be removed; if he refuses to desist from persistence, he is fought as is the rule for the people of rebellion (ahl al-baghy). If two contracts are made simultaneously, by one group for one qualified person and another group for another, and the contracts conflict, then a new contract must be initiated for one of them, or another person qualified for imamate is appointed, like two guardians marrying a minor girl and both contracts occur simultaneously. It is reported from Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi that he said: 'Lots are cast between them, and whoever's lot comes out is the imam.' Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi followed this in his book 'Uyun al-Masa'il. But this is corrupt because it is an arbitration by one whose ignorance is apparent, and it is corrupt. Similarly, casting lots in our school is only permitted for soothing hearts, not for establishing rights. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1111

Commentary
This passage argues against the permissibility of having multiple imams simultaneously and rejects the method of casting lots to resolve disputed claims to the imamate. The author begins by stating that if appointing multiple imams were allowed, it would lead to countless imams in every locality, which would nullify the consensus of the Companions and cause disunity among Muslims, weakening them against enemies. He explains the negative consequences: imams would not obey each other, leading to division and weakness. An exception is noted if a sea barrier prevents mutual support between lands. Then, the author addresses the case of two conflicting contracts for different candidates: the later contract is invalid and the second candidate is a rebel to be fought if necessary. If both contracts occur simultaneously, like the analogy of two guardians marrying a minor girl simultaneously, a new contract must be made. The author refutes Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi and Abu al-Qasim al-Ka'bi's view of using lots, calling it corrupt because it relies on chance from one whose ignorance is manifest, and because lots in Islamic law only serve to appease hearts, not to establish rights. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms
إمام (imam)
إمامة (imamate)
بغي (rebellion)
قرعة (casting lots)
إجماع (consensus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think casting lots is a valid method to decide the imam in disputed cases.
Correction: The author clarifies that lots are only for soothing hearts, not for establishing rights, and the method is corrupt.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if two contracts for imamate are made simultaneously for different people?
A) The older candidate becomes imam.
B) Casting lots decides the imam.
C) A new contract must be initiated for one of them or another qualified person.
D) Both become imams jointly.

Key Takeaway
The imamate must be unified; multiple imams lead to disunity and weakness, and casting lots is a corrupt method for resolving disputes over the imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you seek unity or independence when it comes to leadership? How does this lesson challenge your natural inclination to resist authority or follow your own opinion?
?
When faced with a disputed decision, do you rely on chance (like a coin toss) or do you seek a just, knowledgeable resolution? How does this lesson expose the danger of leaving serious matters to luck?
?
Reflect on a time when you contributed to division rather than unity. What would it cost you to actively pursue unity, even when it means sacrificing your own preference? Will you take one concrete step today towards reconciliation or cooperation?

Refutation of Rawafid and Karramiyya Doctrines on Two Imams
Refutation of Rawafid and Karramiyya views on two imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Rawafid and Karramiyya doctrines on two simultaneous imams are invalid according to Maturidi theology.

Core Idea
Two imams cannot coexist, whether speaking/silent or Sunni/rebelling.

In this passage, the author applies the principle that only one imam can exist at a time to refute two sectarian positions. The Rawafid claim a speaking and silent imam, while the Karramiyya claim that both Ali and Muawiya were legitimate imams for their followers. The author dismisses both as invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Note that the passage begins by declaring both views invalid.
2
Identify the two claims refuted
The passage refutes two groups: Rawafid (claiming two imams, one speaking and one silent) and Karramiyya (claiming Ali and Muawiya as simultaneous imams).
Make sure you understand what 'speaking' and 'silent' imam mean in this context.
3
Understand the basis of refutation
The author does not repeat the argument here but references a previous summary that established the impossibility of two imams. This shows the cumulative nature of theological reasoning.
4
Reflect on the dismissal of Karramiyya
The author calls their statement 'fitting for their ignorance' (لائق بجهالة الكرامية). This is a strong dismissal, showing that the doctrine is not even worth serious argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول الروافض والكرامية في الإمامة باطل] وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة عرف بطلان قول الروافض بثبوت إمامين في وقت أحدهما (٦) ناطق والآخر صامت (۲) ، وبطلان قول الكرامية أن عليًا ومعاوية كانا إمامين على خلاف السنة، وكان يجب على أتباع كل واحد منهما طاعة صاحبهم، والقول الطاعة من خلال السنة لائق بجهالة الكرامية (()، والله الموفق.
[The statement of the Rawafid and the Karramiyya regarding the imamate is invalid.] By understanding this summary, the invalidity of the Rawafid's claim of establishing two imams at one time – one speaking (natiq) and the other silent (samit) – is known, as is the invalidity of the Karramiyya's claim that 'Ali and Mu'awiya were both imams, one in accordance with the Sunna and the other contrary to it, and that the followers of each were obligated to obey their respective leader. The statement of obedience through the Sunna is fitting for the ignorance of the Karramiyya. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 1111

Commentary
This passage refutes two sectarian positions on the imamate. The author first declares the views of the Rawafid and the Karramiyya invalid. He then explains the Rawafid doctrine: they hold that two imams can exist in one time, one 'speaking' (natiq, i.e., active) and the other 'silent' (samit, i.e., passive), referring to al-Hasan and al-Husayn respectively. The author refutes this because earlier arguments (the previous lesson) already established the impossibility of two imams. Next, he refutes the Karramiyya claim that 'Ali and Mu'awiya were both imams simultaneously, with 'Ali being the rightful imam according to the Sunna and Mu'awiya being an imam contrary to the Sunna, yet both were to be obeyed by their followers. The author dismisses this as fitting for the ignorance of the Karramiyya, implying it is baseless. The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

كرامية (Karramiyya)
إمام ناطق (speaking imam)
إمام صامت (silent imam)
باطل (invalid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think two imams can coexist if one is active and the other passive.
Correction: The author refutes this entirely, as only one imam is permissible at any time.

Checkpoint

D) It is a matter of ijtihad.

Key Takeaway
The Rawafid and Karramiyya doctrines on two simultaneous imams are invalid because only one imam can exist at a time.

?
This refutation forces you to choose: only one true imam. In your heart, is your ultimate allegiance to the Prophet's sunna or to a group that claims to follow it but actually deviates? What will you do about it today?
